Dr. Perry’s Pamphlet on Nervous Diseases:
1 Their Cause, Symptoms, and Treatment.
This pamphlet is specially written to show a method of
counteracting the ill effects of indiscretions in early life,
over-work, mental worry, &c., and contains a fund of use-
ful information for every sufferer from the following com-
plaints, viz : —Loss of Energy and Will Power, NERVOUS
DEBILITY, Pains in the Back, Defective Memory, Specks
before the Eyes, Excessive Nervousness, Premature
Decay, Bashfulness, Excessive Heart Palpitation, Des-
pondency, Impure Blood, Liver and Kidney Complaints,
Contagious Diseases, and various other symptoms experi-
enced by Nervous Sufferers.
This Pamphlet is sent Free on Application,
and all in any way suffering from the above would derive
benefit from the perusal of this work.
If the patient’s case is of long standing, and, therefore
in need of treatment, he should lose no time in writing
me a letter detailing in full his symptoms, on receipt of
which I will at once reply, explaining the nature of his
complaint, and if I can effect a cure, the time it will take,
and the cost of doing so. My long experience in the
treatment of Nervous Diseases has made me thoroughly
conversant with all their peculiarities. I am prepared,
therefore, to
Gmnlee a Permanent and Complete Cure in every case I undertake.
I invite the fullest investigation into my qualifications. For some time I was House Surgeon to the Great
Northern Hospital, London, where I had special opportunities of invertigating the causes of Nervous Diseases, and
become perfectly acquainted with the most approved scientific and successful methods of affecting their cure.

This eminent Specialist, Founder, and Chief
Surgeon of the long-established and favourably-
known Dispensary that now bears his name, re-
ceived his medical education in the leading colleges
and hospitals of Europe. He has devoted a life-
time to the study of
PRIVATE, NERVOUS, AND SKIN
DISEASES.
He has by profound research thoroughly investi-
gated their origin, traced their deadly sources and
insidious dissemination, and ascertained the surest
and safest methods by which they may be eradi-
cated, with these widely recognised qualifications
combined with unswerving faithfulness to his
patients, he has during his practice, extending
over FORTY YEARS, attained a well-deserved
pre-eminence in their treatment. His practice
embraces diseases which probably cause more un-
happiness than any other class of troubles that
aillict mankind.
YOUNG MEN
who suffer from the results of early follies, trans-
gressions or excesses of more nature ago, bladder
difficulties, a whitish or dark ropy sediment in
the water, accompanied by slight burning or
smarting sensation, Headaches, Drowsiness during
the day, circles round the eyes, Giddiness,
irregularity of the bowels, specks before the eyes,
bashfulness, discontentedness, weakness or pains
in the back. Liver or kidney complaints, dreams,
buzzing noises in the ears and head, pimples, a
fear that something dreadful is about to happen,
weak stomach, yellow or blood-shot eyes, impure
blood, hasty, uncontrollable temper, hacking
cough, who cannot marry, nervousness in society,
wakefulness, palpitation of the heart, general
weakness, and other symptoms which load to in-
sanity and death, should consult him, for he has
mastered these difficulties. On receipt of 6d. in
stamps he will send secure from observation in a
plain wrapper, postage

SATURDAY, MARCH 12, 1892.
Superfluous Women.
The London Evening Standard, in a recent
issue, is possessed of the seven devils of a
new inspiration. It conceives that Australia
is a favourable held in which to deposit the
surplus women of England, and straightway
it draws up plans and specifications for
exporting the superfluous British female to
these shores, and leaving her there to take
her chance for the future. This idea is
part and parcel of that comprehensive policy
which is summed up in the great Anglo-
Saxon watchword, “ Move on !” When any
single individual in all the three kingdoms
of Great Britain is poor, hungry, homeless,
ragged, diseased, discontented, dirty, or
insane some philanthropist always rises up
with a project for moving him or her on.
The policeman stirs up the shivering vagrant
in the street, and moves him along for his
own good to the next policeman’s beat, and
the next policeman meets him there, and
moves him further on, and so he would pre-
sently be moved right into the North Sea,
only by a special dispensation of Providence
there are gaps between the officers, and the
vagrant gets a chance to dodge in the hiatus
where one policeman ends and the other has
not yet begun. \V hen the Irish famine arose
there arose with it sundry noble-hearted
Christians who, instead of grappling with the
difficulty on the spot, helped to move the
starving peasantry into the United States, and
the best fighting material in the three king-
doms was thus lost to the empire for ever.
The ruined farmers of the Scottish High-
lands have been so effectually moved on to
Canada that very few of that sturdy and
valiant race remain. General Booth, the
Pope with the red shirt and the red nose,
has a scheme for moving the whole of the
ignorant, hopeless, brutish, and utterly
incapable classes of England into Australia
or South Africa or China or Gehenna—it
matters very little where, and the project
has been well received in the British Isles.
And now—as another item in the same plan
the Standard desires that the superfluous
female population of England should be
moved into Australia and left thei’e for our
sympathetic consideration. Every road that
leads from the happy isles of Britain is
trodden hard by the feet of countless
thousands that are always moving on.
* # #
And in every case there is a Paradise just
ahead for the myriads who move on.
Every British philanthropist and newspaper
writer and political economist and Tory
capitalist tells the same story of the fields
of amaranth that will bo reached if the
legion of the lost keeps moving long enough
to make quite sure that it will never, by
any chance, get back. There are schemes
for planting the decayed gentlewomen of
England on the great, scorched, sun-blasted
Avastes of interior Australia, or on the
parched karroos of Southern Africa, or for
depositing broken-down governesses among
the sodden mountains of Central Otago—any
place that is far away is good enough to lie
about in such a connection. And, there-
fore, when the Standard breaks forth like
this, it is fairly in keeping with all the
rest: —
There are thousands of young men want-ins? wives at
this moment in Australia, where there are few wives to
be had. There is a colonial career open to our surplus
female population if they only manage once to get to the
other side of the globe.
We know the kind of women who make up
that “surplus population” which the
Standard desires so much to dispose of.
Out of every thousand females at least two
hundred are ‘ * surplus ” —the oldest and ugliest
two hundred in every case. The elderly,
gaunt governess, with black mittens and
spectacles, the flat chested woman who is no
longer wanted in the factory, the venerable
person with “rheumatics,” the “genteel
spinster” who tries vainly to eke out a pre-
carious existence by embroidery, the helpless
lady who has seen better days, the haggard
boarding-house keeper who is prematurely
old by reason of the unprincipled way the
boarders have escaped by the back window,
thedreary, useless character who keeps a small
shop, the “saved” girl from a “home of hope”
who was reared in an atmosphere of vice,
and can no more shake off the hereditary
taint than she can outlive her own immor-
ality—they are all “surplus,” evei’y one.
The young Australian is alleged to be in
want of a wife, and this gorgon host is
entirely at his service ; the Standard will
cheerfully shunt the whole crowd without a
single exception.
# • •
In Australia and Maoriland, at the present
time, the male population is probably about
250,000 in advance of the female. In most
of the older communities of Europe and
America the female population is consider-
ably ahead, and on this fact the Standard
bases the assumption that there is a great
and incessant demand for women in the
matrimonial market of these provinces. In
other words, it is taken for granted that the
two sexes should be in equal proportion, and
as they are notin equal proportion, therefore
Australia wants women—any kind of them
and any quantity. The theory would hold
good if every man desired to find a partner,
but as it happens he does not. And, on the
other hand, almost every woman does. The
number of men who deliberately prefer to
lead a celibate existence is enormous ; the
number of Avomen avlio so desire can be
counted on the Angers. This circumstance
does not infer that Avomen are fonder
of the married state than men ; it merely
proves .that their necessities are greater.
Civilisation has made this Avorld an ex-
ceedingly cold and inhospitable place
for the spinster avlio possesses no pro-
perty and no male relatives on Avhom
she can levy for her support. In spite of
all that she has gained in recent years the
prospects of the poor and unfriended Avoman
are Avoefully blank—in Australia as Avell as
elseAvhere. Even in those occupations Avhere
she can take her place Avith men, doing the
same Avork and doing it equally well, her
Avages are little more than half those of her
male competitor. The female compositor is
a “blackleg” of necessity ; the union of the
male type-setters refuses to recognise her
existence, and her only chance of employ-
ment is in the office of some unmanly skin-
flint tradesman who Avill engage her because
she is cheap and dare not go on strike. The
tailoress is paid only a fraction of the
tailor’s wages. The Avhole class of sewing
Avomen are worked to the last extremity
that human endurance Avill permit, and
receive about as much in a week as a skilled
male Avorker does in a day. The governess
Avho educates six rampant infants and helps
in the house as Avell receives, probably, some
£35 per annum. That other overdriven
Avretch, the “lady’s companion,” inserts her
plaint daily in the morning papers, to the
effect that she “ will give her services in ex-
change for a comfortable home,” and as
clothes are not mentioned in the compact it
is to be presumed that she wears her old
stock of haggard attire so long as the least
shred of respectability remains attached to it,
and is then “ sacked ” for the sufficient reason
that she is no longer presentable enough for
the situation. And here and there a massive
person makes what is considered a lavish
income by Avashing; in other Avords, she
toils about as hard as a pick-and-shovel
artist, and realises, on an average, 255. per
week as a consequence.
• # •
Domestic service is absolutely the only
female occupation which is not overstocked.
It means practically that the servant shall bo
at the beck and call of a mistress at all hours
of the day and most of the night ; still it is
a comparatively certain resource, and the
payment is better than in most other occu-
pations. But it is unpopular for one suffi-
cient reason : nine-tenths of male humanity
has agreed for many years to look doAvn on
the domestic as an inferior animal—and con-
sequently there is a false sense of utter
degradation attached to the Avork which
nothing can remove. Moreover, half the
friendless women who strive to hang on to
the skirts of the great industrial army have
never learned the Avork of the domestic, and
therefore there remains in every Australian
city—as in every other city the Avhole Avorld
over—a great, floating mass of AvidoAvs and
spinsters, whose means of livelihood is a
mystery. Some among them become barmaids,
and of these a few wear out early in the glare
of the gas and the evil odour of stale cigars and
ancient beer-slops, and are turned adrift
when they have lost their comeliness ; and
a few more elope with some flash bar-loafer
and live more or less happily ever after-
wards ; and a very small section—the wise
virgins of the trade—lie low till the landlady
dies, and then marry the landlord, and when
he ends his career through apoplexy, they
inherit the business and give up marriage.
Some more, less fortunate, advertise in the
Evening News for some “kind, elderly
gent ” who will lend £5 to a lady in great
distress. Here and there one gets some
precarious clerical work, but these are
exceedingly few ; an advertisement offering
255. a week, without board, for any descrip-
tion of copying or semi-literary employment
will, within 48 hours, bring from 300 to 500
answers in Melbourne or Sydney, all from
women who write and speak like ladies, and
wear darned gloves, and carry shabby
umbrellas, and who are distinguished by
that anxious, weary look which comes of
much seeking after situations and finding
them not.
• • •
And they are all, to the last woman, or at
least to the last woman but one, willing to
get married. Marriage is a lottery, but,
uncertain as it is, it is still much more reli-
able than that other lottery which is repre-
sented by the list of vacant situations in the
S.M. Herald or Age. Even the “agony
column” of the daily paper bears witness
to this; the man who advertises for a
wife will receive six times as many genuine
responses as the woman who advertises for a
husband. Women are already a drug in the
market in Australia, despite the fact that
they are in a minority. Under present social
conditions, when any country contains two
women to every three men, it has as many
as can be sufficiently provided for. When
the proportion grows larger then there begins
a process of natural—or unnatural—selection
whereby the gaunt and ugly woman always
gets left. There is no place on earth in this
nineteenth century for the ugly woman.
Even in Australia, where the female sex is
probably more scarce than in any other part
of the globe, there is no man so hairy and
shapeless, so much bowed in the legs and
gone in the spine, so objectionable in man-
ners, language, or appearance, that he cannot
get a wife, while the chances of a seriously
plain woman getting a husband are exceed-
ingly limited. Even a distorted executioner
can always find a bride in this land of pros-
perity ; in England, which is many shades
worse, a gorilla, provided the animal has
any visible means of support, can choose
from among a dozen or more, probably a
hundred, of the opposite sex, all willing to
take any risk in order to get away from the
grinding poverty of modern industrialism.
* # #
There is no room in Australia for the
superfluous women of England. There is
no room for them anywhere, unless the
world’s goods are differently distributed so
as to give to each human being a reasonable
chance of sustenance. Even if all men were
able and willing to marry it would only make
the problem worse by causing an abnormal in-
crease of population in a world which, under
existing social conditions, is already over-
populated. For though the earth can furnish
necessaries and even luxuries for twice its
present population, the one incontrovertible
fact remains that it does not do so, and w ith
every increase in the census returns the
aggregate misery grows worse and still worse.
Either a remedy must be found by a com-
plete reorganisation of society, so that Dives
and Lazarus will both be abolished—or else
some day the law must begin to weed out
the superfluous population by some process
of judicial murder, and must kindly brain
the outcasts, male and female alike, for
whom it refuses to supply food and shelter.
In that case England must settle her own
butcher’s bill, in common with the rest of
the ivorld. The old policy of “ move on ”
the mean device whereby she exports her
insoluble problems and then curses other
nations because they can find no solution for
them—has done good service for three cen-
turies, but it will not answer its purpose
much longer.
Marriage and Divorce.
Dear Bulletin, What is Marriage ? Is
it a simple contract entered into by a
man and a woman for mutual convenience
and happiness, or is it a Goo-given something
lying altogether outside the region of prac-
tical life ? Are the Roman Catholics right
in their refusal to sanction any and every
description of annulment of the conjugal tie ?
Is it better for people unhappily wedded to live
in adultery, to practise promiscuity, or by
means of legal divorce to re-wed under happier
auspices? The theological objector bases an
argument on a text: “ What God hath joined
together let no man put asunder.” We might
point out in passing that the injunction is in
itself equivocal ; levelled rather at the stealer
of another’s wife. The Divorce Court Judge is
powerless to separate man and wife; they
themselves alone are able to do that. The law
simply ratifies and registers the separation.
• • •
Disease is evolutionary. Every society has
the amount of crime that it deserves. No
plague can flourish unless conditions are favour-
able to its growth. Influenza is the scourge of
thc./f n-de-sitch. Nobody knows much about it.
When it has disappeared and social conditions
have altered for the accommodation of another
disease more in consonance with them, influqnza
will be ticketed and classified, and everybody
will know everything—or nothing—about it
Marriage is very similar. It is vot regul fttef j
by society ; it regulates society. It is an insti.
tution of slow and painful growth. Man p
naturally polygamous. In primeval days l le
chose his mate for a season, and the conju<r a i
tie was of the slenderest. As lie congregated in
societies with his kind, he began to acquj le
property. Still polygamous, he exercised th e
rights of absolute proprietorship over his wives
and the tally of his wealth was the size 0 {
his harem. Little by little in the more
advanced societies monogamy was evolved, but
it had to fight hard to retain its place, and'man
took many revenges on his partner for the sur-
render of his right to marry as many wives as
he chose. Religion aided the fixing of outward
form, and the marriage-rites and the matri-
monial ideals of races were fossilised in the
codes of reformers like Mahomet. Rut the
evolution of marriage and of the social position
of woman has been going on notwithstand-
ing. V\ e have long ceased to regard
woman as mere property ; with the grow'th of
Socialism we are beginning to outgrow' our con-
ception of woman as a medium for the pre-
servation of property. With the last expiring
notion of the right of private ownership of
land and the sanctity of landlordism, the idea
of the sanctity of the legal marriage will also
disappear. The conception of such a thing as
the “sanctity” of marriage is only a rider on
the conception of such“ a thing as the
“reproach” of bastardy. With the destruc-
tion of the landlord, illegitimacy will become a
dream, and the Divorce Court will extend its
ladder of successive flights of steps from the
rigidity of the priest-knotted union to the
elysium of free conjugal contract.
* # #
All divorce extension enactments tend to the
abolition of marriage. It is, in fact, the Anglo-
Saxon method of correcting the mistakes of the
original contract. Woman clamours for the
franchise, but the franchise will fail to make
her politically the equal of man unless she is
socially and sexually his equal, and this
equality she is learning to seek through an
extension of the scope of the divorce laws.
Political equality of the sexes is impossible
while one sex is conjugally subject to the
other ; and blink the matter as we may, the
solution of the struggle for equality will be
found in the total abolition of state-recognised
marriage. It is purely a question of the
development of Socialism. The marriage ideal
which has grown out of actual buying and sell-
ing rights over a wdfe cannot stand before the
tests applied by the New Order. Woman moves
slowly along the socialistic plane. Her first
great triumph was the popular recognition of
the principle of monogamy. To-day she is
demanding the franchise. To-morrow she will
demand release from all marital control and
absolute liberty of conjugal contract. We cannot
say that this is ethically right; nor can we say
that it is wrong. It is, however, the trend of
modern sociology. A little while since Mrs.
J ulian Ashton read a paper before the Sydney
branch of the Women’s Suffrage League in
which she railed on modern marriage in good
set terms, and raised thereby the active opposi-
tion of the entire shortsighted sisterhood, wdio,
although clamouring for voting equality with
man, do not recognise that such equality implies
absolute social and sexual responsibility for
woman. Mrs. Ashton simply put in a plea for
freedom of the matrimonial contract, and in
doing this she was unconsciously urged onward
by the spirit of her time. People cannot
absorb Ibsen and Tolstoi, Mona Cairo and
Paul BouroEt without being alternately dis-
gusted with the modern marriage and childishly
enthusiastic for future freedom. Separation,
like Federation, is in the air, and nothing can
be done for woman until nothing is done for
her—she must settle the problem of her own
position, incited thereto by the prophets of the
New Order.
# * #
What, however, is marriage? The union of
a man and a woman in perfect sexual fidelity
for a period—for life say the Roman Catholics.
In this, then, exists the “sanctity” of mar-
riage, in the perfect sexual fidelity ? The mere
ceremony is not marriage, the clerically-blessed
vows, the mystical mummery ; the only thing
worthy the name is the preservation of a
plighted troth in its integrity. Granted ; but
are not many men, united by the ceremonial of
the Church, false to their vows of conjugal
constancy ? Are not many who have never
taken such vows true to the one love unblessed
by priest or parson ? Which is the marriage
here ? If the Church refuses to dissolve the
loveless union, will men and women not live
openly in the free union without the sanction of
the Church ? The question of immorality
involved is a question for ecclesiasticism to
answer. When the Church refuses or is unable
to toe the chalk-line of progress her influence
immediately begins to ebb. The other day the
Pope had an opportunity of which he failed to
make the most. He might have headed, and
stemmed also, the socialistic movement —he
condemned it. He denounced the present con-
dition of things, but he refused to sanction any
other. When the cheering has subsided wdiich
hails the supreme Pontiff’s “sympathy with
Labour,” it will be found that the influence of
Rome has lapsed two centuries. Ere the Pope
spoke, everything was possible. He has spoken,
and his word stands infallibly committed. In
another decade men will critically compare the
utterances of the successor of St. Peter with
the dreams of ages, and the multitude of voices
of even the present century. As in the social
movement so in marriage. The sandal worn by
the saint of the keys is too small for the foot of
this latter day. Protestantism has protested
once, hence its influence is dead for ever. Its
benison or its malison helps or hurts not a whit.
The voice of the Nonconformist clergyman, or
of the bishop of the Established Church, is no
holier than the voice of Bradlaucjh or of
Huxley. With Catholicism it is different.
The antiquity of the Church is alone a saving
power. The splendid roll of its achievements,
its gloriously human side, its active philan-
throphy, its .esthetic sensuousness, its literature,
its arts, and its traditions—all these are claims
on the cars of men to be heard, and heard
loudly when aught is uttered that touches
keenly the business and interests of the people.

* * *
The modern marriage is doomed ! Its death
y ] 3e a slow one, but every wound hastening
it will he received through a legislative enact-
ment enlarging the operation of the divorce laws.
The “ saptity ” of marriage will be, is being,
utterly ground to powder by the iconoclasm of
divorce,'ami in this work of destruction female
suffrage will play no insignificant part. It may
bo the best or the worst thing that ever hap-
pened to woman, but it will happen. It is
ridiculous to hold women themselves as respon-
sible for this new trend of modern thought.
They arc obedient only to a blind force which
is leading them they know not whither. The
woman movement is part and parcel of the trades-
union movement and the social movement. The
at ack on landlordism, the advocacy of land-
ir.lionalisation, the demand for confiscatory
probate duties, the arraignment of the law of
inheritance, are successive blows dealt at the
nyves of matrimony. The union of the future
will and must be a union of sentiment, just as
the union of the present is of necessity a union
of interest; but iu order that sentiment may
realise its own demands it will tolerate no
restriction and recognise no authority but its
own. There may be a lot of danger in all this
for Humanity, but Humanity has weathered
worse perils. One thing is, however, un-
doubtedly a danger of an extreme character,
f aught with very serious consequences, and
1 1 is is the unphilosophical attempts put forward
by sentimental idealists to curb, crib, coffin and
coniine a purely natural and necessary develop-
ment. They may do so for a season, but it only
adds to the difficulties of ultimate adjustment.
They appeal to the “sanctity” of marriage;
hut the word “sanctity” is only a word, and
in the present connection it died in the mediaeval
aje-. -B.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Cheap Labourer on Strike.
Tub Italian immigration scheme in Queensland
is already showing signs of collapse, and in its
collapse another woo is added to the troubles of
the province. The Roman organ-grinder is on
strike, and the country has only the satisfaction
of knowing that it has imported, at the expenseof
the overburdened taxpayer, several hundred un-
employed in addition to the great army already
inexistence. As the event proves, this cheap alien
is much loss fitted than the Australian to work
in the malarious atmosphere of the tropical
cmic-tields. The one and only reason for his
existence in Queensland is his alleged capacity
for toiling 12 hours per diem for an infinitesimal
pittance per week and a microscopic ration,
and now that this alleged capacity is utterly
disproved, there is no reason for him at all.
All over Central Queensland gangs of destitute
and desperate Italians are tramping south-
ward, ready to work at half wages and thus
tut down the pay of the distressed Austra-
lian still lower ready to beg, borrow or
steal, or to, do anything, in fact, save go back
to labour at less than Chinese rates on the
Northern plantations. Most of them will pre-
Sently vanish among the slums of Brisbane
and Sydney, and help to swell the chronic
demand for relief works or public charity, and
their places will bo taken by fresh swarms of
Italians who will be sifted through the planter’s
sieve in like manner, and then go out to be a
airdcu on the community at large. The country
pays the. freight upon these strangers, the planter
yets probably one month’s work out of them, and
'en the nation has to support them for ever

A Melbourne Weed.
Negotiations have been pushed to the point of
holding public meetings of interested share-
holders with a view to the amalgamation of
three Melbourne daily papers — Daily Tele-
graph (morning) and the Herald and the
Evening Standard. Up to date, these efforts
have been unsuccessful. The consummation of
the proposed amalgamation is to herald a
newspaper cable monopoly and further em-
bastion the position of the anti-derao-
cratic press. The three papers thus trying
to become absorbed into one are, broadly
speaking, “ birds of a feather.” Each has been
running its own little rig against the interests
of the democracy : not one is an exponent of
the real sentiments of the people. Melbourne
Daily Telegraph has been run by a politi-
cally poisonous, and, in some other respects,
also venomous, nest of bigoted, wealthy
pietists, grim and bleak as the land from which
most of them made their descent upon Austra-
lia. The Herald, as a variant of a similar
type, has been under Irish control, and,
being Irish, it became its constant care to
save its consequent reputation by roaring
out its effusive loyalty upon the slightest pro-
vocation or excuse ; and it has succeeded in
exhibiting itself upon a thousand occasions as a
very ground-worm among newspapers. The
Evening Standard is a vapour of puff and
grovel, which howls “ God save the Queen ” all
the time, and is fathered by a crowd, at whose
head is that rich vanity-monger, Simon Eraser,
the individual who commissioned Millais to
paint his portrait for a sum, variously stated at
amounts ranging from £lOOO to £3OOO. Mel-
bourne has not one single daily newspaper that
does not “ go kronk” on every important demo-
cratic question. The Age, the biggest “ demo-
cratic” fraud by which a free and presumably
intelligent people ever permitted itself to be
gulled, has, on countless occasions, deliberately
betrayed the masses, and is betraying them
now ; but it is a cheering sign of the sound-
ness of the Victorian democracy’s core, that
occasional nnmistakeable evidence proves that
the glamour once exercised by the Age is a
lost and ridiculed pretension. Labour-Member
Hancock demonstrated this when he stormed
the working-man’s electorate of Collingwood in
the teeth of the publicly-blasted thunder of that
once mighty organ. If it were possible to
make a papier-mdche politician out of the Age,
Judas Iscariot could be resurrected and fully
identified. The Argus is always opposed to
the people, but it makes no virtuous pretence
that it is not run in the interests of a class.
The Argus is, in fact, the organ of “ the vicar
and the squire,” so far as those English institu-
tions can be parodied in Australia. Mel-
bourne’s present journalistic need is a straight-
out, radical, republican paper, not necessarily
competing in size and expenditure with the
amalgamated triplet, when that ■wonderful
combination does take place, but with an
integrity above bribery, and always run by men
whose pens shall drip the gall and wormwood
that is really in the writers’ hearts. There is
a gigantic opening in Melbourne for two or
three sincere radicals, intellectually original
enough to aerate the furnaces of democracy,
and fearless enough to perform to its omega the
journalistic duties they would, in such instance,
take upon their own shoulders. There is a
magnificent democratic battle to be fought in
the South, and, even though the present daily
Press of that province’s metropolis is arrayed
against the people’s cause all up the gamut of
manhood’s aspirations, the great bulk of the
Victorian population is quite sound on demo-
cratic questions. It needs but a magnetic
popular leader to strike the key-notes. Vic-
torian politicians are now overborne by the big
newspapers. It Premier Shiels is a Radical,
he dare not attempt to give effect to his views.
Did another Berry arise and awaken the
slumbering volcanoes in the united mind of the
Victorian masses, he would, despite the plural
vote, sweep the polls victoriously from Wilson’s
Promontory to Mildura, and from Casterton to
Croajingolong. At present, the inner demo-
cracy of Victoria has no exponential expression.
The Logic of Monopoly.
The following is published in Sydney Echo. It
is merely a letter from a correspondent in the
West, but the Echo cheerfully inserts it under
the heading “Scandalous Outrages in Queens-
land,” and, by so doing, distinctly endorses the
statements and accepts them as its own :
“The firestick brigade has been at work at Cameron
Downs. Six men well known to the police, travelling
from Hughenden towards Muttaburra, lighted the grass
on the road in many places. A police patrol saw the men
on the road, and later saw the fires, but could not convict
the inoendiarists. Kain fell soon after, and no serious
damage was done. 1 only report the matter to you, says
Mr. Crombib, “in order that you may be in a position to
advise the authorities of the scoundrelism which is wan-
dering about and waiting any opportunity which may
offer to reduce Central Queensland to an ash-heap. Our
manager offered £lOO reward for evidence that would
secure a conviction, but so far without result. It is sup-
posed these are the revenge committee, or a portion of
them, who some time ago issued a threatening manifesto
from Hughenden. They are travelling this way, and
would probably have given us all a turn if the rain had
not spoilt their game.”
The generous and honourable monopolist shines
forth conspicuously in this effusion. Every
summer bush lires break out and rage furiously
in almost all parts of Australia, and particularly
in the North, where the sun is hottest and the
grass is driest. They were prevalent before
the white man first invaded the country, and
the earliest explorers passed through forests of
blackened stumps, and traversed great wastes
of ashes and desolation. When the country is
at peace, these conflagrations are set down to
chance or accident, or any other impersonal
influence ; when there is dissension between
Capital and Labour, they are ascribed in
every case to the brutal, bloodthirsty and
revolutionary shearer. This year there have
been much fewer fires than usual, Imt at every
separate blaze the monopolist howls for
vengeance on the labourer, and despatches
frantic telegrams about an imaginary “ lirestick
brigade ” —which is almost as logical as if he
demanded that the shearer should be sent to
gaol for drought, or scab, or pleuro-pneumonia.
In the present instance it appears that six men
were travelling along a road and there was a
fire. There is generally someone travelling
along a road, and there is just as much visible
or necessary connection between the two events
as if “ Mister James ” Fairfax had walked
down Pitt-street, Sydney, and a Chinaman had
had a fit. If the fact of the aforesaid men
being on the Muttaburra road proves that they
created the fires, then the fact of Mr. Fairfax
being in Pitt-street proves that he caused the
Mongol to have the fit. Nobody possesses any
vestige of evidence to show that the aforesaid
individuals caused the conflagration, or that it
arose from any other influences than those
which led to previous conflagrations when the
land was still an empty wilderness; but all
the same the Echo cheerfully describes them as
“ incendiarists ” and “ the revenge committee.”
Yet what a storm there would be in the office
of that moral paper if some other journal
published an equally logical and Christian
paragraph like this :
The fire-fiend is at work. The proprietor of the Echo, a
person well known to the police, was seen travelling down
Sussex-street, and immediately afterwards a fire was dis-
covered in the back premises of Mrs. Jane Smith. Unfor-
tunately, there is no evidence to convict the loathsome
incendiary who evidently wants to reduce this city to an
ash-heap. A reward of £lOO has been offered for his con-
viction, but as yet without avail.
The two cases are absolutely identical, even to
the fact of both parties being “ well known to
the police.” The proprietor of the Echo is
well known to these functionaries inasmuch as
they almost all recognise him when he passes
by. The six base miscreants on the Mutta-
burra road were also “well-known”—in fact,
the “cop” who is stationed for any length of
time in a lonely Queensland township knows
everybody, including the local preaching man
and the infuriated squatter-person whose grass
took fire. Therefore, the fact proves no more
than the circumstance of the Chief Justice
being known to the Governor demonstrates
that the former is the cause of the Windsor
murder. The contention of the Echo simply
amounts to this : When any disaster happens
to a rich man, any poor man who happens to
be within 10 or 20 miles of the trouble can be
lawfully inserted in a grovelling newspaper
under the designation of “Scandalous Outrage.”
Of such materials is the logic of Monopoly con-
structed.

The New South Wales Cheap Labour
Bureau.
That noble institution, the Government Labour
Bureau, of Sydney, pursues the even tenor of
its way, and quietly and unobtrusively—after
the manner of all truly benevolent organisations
—it has already done much to diminish the
chronic suffering of the New South Wales
capitalist. There is a great falling-off in the
number of unemployed ; the seedy men with
the haggard eyes, who are always looking for
work, have been sent up country, and things
begin to assume a more promising aspect.
Whenever a back-blocks employer has a diffi-
culty with his men because they object to work
longer hours with a corresponding reduction of
wages, he at once telegraphs to the Labour
Bureau, and the Government despatches a sutli-
cient number of labourers on the free-pass
system to undersell those who have been locked
out, and thus the number of unemployed in the
city is daily diminishing. Some of these men
have declined to go blacklegging at the expense
of others, and are now walking back, hungry
and laden with curses ; others accepted the
work, and in these cases it is the men whom
they supplanted who are walking back instead.
But till they arrive things look better in the
city of Sydney. The demand for labour, since
the Bureau commenced operations, has been
enormous. Nearly every employer in the far
West and South and North started to
reduce wages w'henever he heard of that
noble institution, and when his employes
objected, he sent in a requisition for men—who
were promptly sent up at the country’s expense
—to take their places. Most of these were
unionists, but even the most ardent unionist is
very likely to go back on his principles when
a paternal Government has dropped him down
in a howling wilderness 600 miles from his
home, and he must either turn renegade or
walk back, with no earthly prospects at the
end of his journey. It is a simple and effective
inode of conversion; if Pabkes or G. H. Reid
were deposited in a penniless state at (say)
Wilcannia, with the option of turning Protec-
tionists or getting home on foot without any
reasonable hope of victuals en route, they would
turn Protectionists then and there, both of them.
There is no demand for labour at decent wages
in the country districts any more than in the
city ; every man who is sent out only deprives
another man of work, and when the Bureau
finds billets for 400 unemployed, and proudly
mentions the fact in its report, it has simply
made 400 more unemployed—who are not men-
tioned in the report —in their stead. Then the
400 who have lost their work through the
agency of the nefarious institution will gravi-
tate by degrees, and at much cost of shoe-
leather, back to the city; and when their old
employers make a further reduction of wages,
and their successors go on strike, they will be
shipped back at the country’s cost to the billets
which they held before, and the Government
will announce that another great squadron of
poor and deserving men have been provided for,
and 400 more labourers will commence the
eternal process of walking back. The only man
for whom the Bureau has found a genuine
billet, and who isn’t walking back, is the
manager, Mr. Joseph Greer, whose salary
goes on regularly, whatever happens. The
institution has found work for one unemployed ,
anyhow. $
Even the Mackay Standard, a special cham-
pion of sugar-planters, holds that Premier
Griffith lias no constitutional right to reverse
the deliberately expressed policy of the electors
in respect of black labour, without first rc-sub-
mitting the question to those electors. It
maintains that newsnaner contributions and
press leaders form no warrant for Griffith’s
proposed course of action, and says that “there
is obviously a constitutional method of pro-
cedure which even (!) Sir S. W. Griffith dare
not depart from without laying himself open to
the condemnation of those lovers of law and
order of whom he now declares, himself the
champion.” This is healthy talk, although the
Mackay Standard advocates an immediate
election as likely to result in a popular endorse-
ment of Griffith’s MTlwraithian policy.
Brisbane Telegraph has definitively pro-
nounced against the resuscitation of black
labour, and prefers rather to see the
total extinction of the sugar industry
than the revival of the colour ferment.
As Brisbane Telegraph always tries to jump
with the cat of public opinion, this also is a
cheering sign. The Toowoomba Chronicle,
the newspaper owned by William Henry
Groom, whom Griffith, after a hot fight,
landed in the Speaker’s chair in ’B3, also opposes
the black-labour proposals, tooth and nail.
Labour member Glassey is probably right in
his prophecy that there are enough anti-black
members in the Bananaland Assembly to compel
Griffith, whether he likes it not, to appeal to
the country on both his nigger proposals and
his (or rather MTlwraith’s) “trans-continen-
tal ” land-grant railway scheme. W. 0. Hodg-
kinson, Minister for Mines, and Griffith’s
champion apologist, has more than hinted that
his own idea of a compromise is to indent
coloured labour from British New Guinea,

The old Queensland “ labour-trade ” among the
islands was one constant record of kidnapping
and murder at sea, and of black men dying like
rotten sheep on the plantations. Interspersed
with these events there were a few instances
when the slave-hunters quarrelled amongst
themselves, and then the older and more astute
ruffians of the blackbirding service generally
“informed” upon some green and harmless
victim who had rashly entered his protest
against their methods, and got him penal servi-
tude for life, the first five years in irons. The
islanders who died were happier than those who
returned, for nobody kept any very accurate
record of where they came from, and the kindly
captain and his pal, the drunken Government
agent, often simply dropped them among any
hostile tribe that was handy, and sailed away.
Now that these old horrors are about to start
afresh Bishop Selwyn, of Melanesia, suggests
(it is cabled) that the Government itself sliould
conduct the service, in order to prevent the
“ recuiting ” of children without the consent of
their parents, and other abominations. It
would be interesting to know how the Govern-
ment could conduct such a service, so as to
make it respectable. It must pay by results or •
it won’t get any niggers, and if it pays by
results wholesale kidnapping becomes a dead
certainty. • (
As to murderer Bertrand’s proposed release
after being 26 years in a New South Wales gaol
(from his 24th to his 50th year), the case stands
thus : No other sane murderer has ever been
locally imprisoned so long. Bertrand is
believed to be still in gaol only because of
certain entries concerning a lady (related to a
prominent powerful official) found in his diary.
This theory may or may not be untrue, but the
fact remains that the convict has been treated
in a most markedly exceptional way. The
Bulletin regards capital punishment as a
hoary and useless fraud upon civilisation, and
holds that every fairly-convicted cold-blooded
murderer should be imprisoned for life. But
fish should not be made of one and flesh of
another. Many dangerous ruffians reprieved
from the gallows long after Bertrand’s con-
viction have been at large for years.
+ —.—.
Why is it that judges, alone among all the ser-
vants of the State, should be so exceedingly
subject to lachrymose emotion ? At frequent
intervals we read that some occupant of the
bench passed sentence in a broken utterance, or
granted a decree nisi with tears in his eyes.
The latest instance of this weakness is the case
of Judge Windeyer, who made an order in the
Bradley matrimonial case “ with a trembling
break in his voice and a visage expressive of
strong feeling.” The matter was principally a
plain circumstance concerning alimony, and
there is nothing specially tearful about alimony
to account for the outbreak. Judges seem to
have a monopoly of this sort of thing ; their
work is not much more affecting than that of
other public functionaries, but they don’t seem
to bear up like the rest of the service. We
never read of how Mr. See was moved while
bringing in his financial statement, or
that John Hogg, road-mender, commenced
with deep emotion to repair the highway in the
vicinity of Goff’s Harbour. The present writer
went gold-hunting in the days of his stormy
youth, but no official burst into tears while
taking the money for his miner’s right. Nobody
ever saw a policeman weeping as he hauled a
burglar to the lockup, though he, equally with
the Judge, is a representative of the law, and
a Government Astronomer seldom breaks down
while looking for a new comet, nor does the
hangman ever weep while tying his clients’ arms
at the elbows. The Judge runs this branch of
business all by himself. "He reminds us of the
gentle, maudlin novel of the early part of this
century, in which the small, pale, gentle
heroine wept bitterly at least once on each
page. The public grew tired of that heroine in
course of time hy reason of her weeping, and
they will get tired of the sentimental occupant
of the bench for the same reason. This country
wants dry Judges.
They say now Rev. Fitchett is actually
returning to London, to help in editing the Re-
view of Reviews. Well, he’s clever enough for
anything, this quarryman turned parson. Ho
struck oil with his ‘“X Y Z” papers, in the
Melbourne Spectator, but the odium theologicum,
professional jealousy, actually got them stopped,
or rather the very heavy lead of the Rev. Dough-
nut Buncombe was substituted for Fitchett’s keen
rapier. Fitchett used to write fiercely in the
Southern Cross, against people going to church
in carriages, but on returning from England, the
first time, he drove a natty buggy, with a pony
pair, to his Sunday preaching appointments.
Only a change of opinion—like Sam Griffith on

816 Macquarie-slreet North, Sydney, March 9,1892.
M v Dear Moorabinda,—
A great rush of decollette to the dress circle was observ-
able at the one-night revival of “ Lucia di Lammeimoor”
at the Royal last Saturday, and every seat in the house
was taken. Mayoress Manning had a party in the prompt
d.c. box, and wore a black gown with jet about it (by the
way, black gowns with jet about them were worn by
every second woman, and they made the d.c. look as if
it wanted blotting); Miss Manning, in black, trimmed
with coppery pink. Mrs. Simpson, in a black gown with
crimson feather fan, had her daughter with her, wearing
red veiling and brocade. Mrs. Bland Holt, fresh from
hygienic Tasmania, sported a brown lace and silk gown,
trimmed with bronze beads, diamond ornaments ; and a
musical lady from Manly was funeralish in silver tinsel
cloth and black lace. Apropos, though the whole house
rose and shrieked rapturously at Cuttica, only one little
flora,! offering fluttered over the footlights. Theatrical
applause is sometimes like spurious friendship—it can’t
be distinguished from the, genuine thing until you ask it
to put its hand in its pocket.
The wardrobe behind the curtain was mostly Scotch, ye
ken. In the first scene tha lark-like Cuttica (itinerant
habitues of Covent Garden unearthed in local dress circles
bracket her with Patti) was a sonsie lassie enough in a
cream and cardinal checked short skirtlet with black
velvet bodice and Glengarry cap and tartan socks, com-
plete in which Hielan’ get-up she looked like the younger
sister of the “ Lucia ” w ho sallied forth in the next act in
a. white satin trained skirt with girdle studded with stage
diamonds ; but I hope no advanced virgin will look upon
this as a rejuvenation tip, and come simpering out
with half a tuck between her bony knees and an un-
charitable world, in consequence. In the vocally difficult
mad scene, a white chiffon robe-de-chambre tied with
white satin ribbons was sported with much effect, but
how Lucia could act as she did against a depressing back-
ground of apparently petrified supes is an enigma—why
can’t the choristers see that it isn’t natural to stand
simpering with your hands folded in “stage direction”
manner while a lunatic dances in an alcove with a
dagger? And, speaking of the human padding, it looked
odd to see the huntsmen in the first scene all retainers of
Enrico, and, therefore, all liable to the same complaint in
plaid, each in a kilt of different tartan, one daring spirit
draped in Rob Roy ! Also “Arturo,” a little “ slipped ”
in the knee and got up in pink and lavender satin with
lace round his perky knickerbockers, was the most awful
warning one could desire to frighten the foppish youths
who want to introduce a few rainbows into their conven-
tional black and white dress-suit.
Apropos of masculine fal-de-rals, why is it that an
Italian or a Frenchman can wear a sgventeenth-century
suit and look as happy and natural as if he put it on every
morning when he got up, while an Englishman, trimmed
with tinsel and lace, looks as if he had just dropped in to
apply lor the job hearing that the other scarecrow had
got the sack? The same with vocalisation. The average
“farriner”, warbles as if the erotic melodies had that
moment just occurred to him, hut the average Englishman
throws sentiment off his chest parrotishly, as if he were
dying to explain between verses, “ Put all the tom-foolery
down to the composer— l’ve had no hand in it.”
‘ ‘ Un Balio in Maschera ” ran a brief two nights at Sydney
Royal, with Guidotti (her stage method of locomotion
looks as if she was hiccoughing with both feet) as the fair
co-respondent; her wardrobe seemed down to low water-
mark, judging by the fact that she sported one long-tailed
black veivet-and-moire garment for three solid acts. And
the worst of it was that the garment was the same sar-
torial shriek in which she saw “ Elvira” and “ Leonora ”
through. Of course, in grand opera trills, and not
toilettes, are the things you go for, but it’s cuttingit too
fine to put up three tragic females in one frock, for all
the world as if they were members of one operatic tribe
with an hereditary rag handed down like the family
diamonds. In the fifth act, Amelia was dressed in puce
satin, the front criss-crossed with gold cord, but the lady
was so smothered in what looked like a lace robe-de-nuit
and nightcap en suite that the full dazzle of her array
didn’t get into the limelight. Dolly-wigged Mattioli was
a well-dressed Oscar—her last suit of blue-and-gold look-
ing like a Masonic emblem and a bon-bon.
On dit that a recently-deceased personage (has left his
daughters a thousand each as a remembrancer—but on
the principle of the more you have the more you grab at
there is some grumbling, of course. By the way, his
youngest daughter flew in the face of a big settlement
over 10 years ago by marrying someone on whose account
pa threatened to cut her off with a blessing. The paternal
consent to the union was given very trickily. The suitor
had harassed him for some time (his religious convictions
wouldn’t let him marry against the wisli of a rich parent,
you bet), and at last pa gave in. But after the marriage-
cereraony the dear old parent blandly told the palpitating
pair—“ I gave you my consent because I knew my
daughter’s -er—obstinacy—hut the marriage is against
my will, and you won’t get a penny when I’m dead.” Bub
they did, for she shares equally with the rest.
Which reminds me that the same old gentleman always
kept a keen eye on his domestic expenditure until he was
100 donc-up to add two and two. Iq his vigorous days he had
a decided “down” on file laundress and invariably
suspected that misconstrued female of appropriating his
linen. To make her shake in her guilty shoes he, always
counted his washing under her very nose, carefully com-
paring the returns with a list of .the output which he
religiously kept. That is the sort of man who will always
run into six figures.
How do you account for the fixed manner in which
the Australian society girl now travels all the way to
London to he married—dragging the happy man with
her, though, of course, he always goes by another tub?
In Sydney at least the top layer girl seems to have a
craven fear of giving up the single ghost in a local church,
and one vaguely wonders why these roving marriages are
so popular. One young lady who fled to England to
shake off the virginal coil had good reason, for there was
an old lover looming in the background swearing that he
would put in his claim—“ aye, at the very altar-rails ”
and she got away to England just to checkmate the
vindictive person ; but surely that motive doesn’t actuate
all these wandering couples? Is it natural for a girl to
flee away from kith and kin and the admiring women
who would hop a mile and a quarter to get a good
seat at the ceremony—not to mention the two-column
newspaper comments on the proceedings in general from
the breakfast to the colour of the bride’s stockings—just
to be married in a cold English church with no gueslsi
and the dejeuner set out in some gilded pub.? It can’t be
that all society brides have a gloomy past that threatens
to make a scene in front of the parson, and it can’t be
that the blushing leading lady prefers to undergo the
matrimonial metamorphosis among strangers. In the
interests of caterers, frockists and the local clergy there
ought to be a clause in the ITotectionist policy enforcing
a heavy duty on all such matrimonial exports ; don’t you
think ?
Hearing all about “fashionable” Australian weddings,
one cannot help remarking how the old barriers against
moneyed trade are being pulled down in this age of
cynicism and creature-comfort, when fathers who, not
so longer ago, would sooner have given their gal to a
sweep than a shopman, gladly hand her over to young
Mr. Tailor’s Goose, yea—and boast of him afterwards !
Time was when the particular haughty parent that I
have in my mind’s eye, Horatio, turned up the nose that
had been in his family for aeons at a much more
distangay alliance than the one to which he now gives
his smiling sanction. The moral to all this seems to be
that it’s best to marry Matilda to the first respectable
bidder, because you never know how long her complexion
will last. Your daughter may be peach-cheekcd and
eleven-stone this year; next year she may be a gal of
nine-stone-four, with an apricot-coloured skin, who looks
particularly old when she laughs. It must be a dreadful
thing to have a woman like that about a house. “Small
profits and quick returns’’—that is the latter-day
matrimonial motto.
“ How does Lady Jersey treat you ?” loftily enquired a
lady (of the female deputation variety) of a comrade, who
in reply sadly and enviously confessed that she and
M.E.J. weren’t chummy, at which her antagonist
smiled in an inner-circle manner before proceeding to
enlarge upon her intimate relations with our Figurehead.
And the little scene set me thinking of the myriads of
women there are whose sole ambition is to buttonhole
Lady J. on some pretext or another and then talk about
it for the rest of their natural lives. Women who rat-tat
at Government House to beg patronage for a flannel
petticoat contest, carry on afterwards as if they and Lady-
Jersey were boon comrades, while next-door listens mouth
open. Then there is the pushing philanthropist who
wood-pecks at the sacred portal to draw M.E.J.’s atten-
tion to the virtues of the horns for stray sheep— and she
goes away after wasting an hour or two of the vice-regal
morning, in a paroxysm of gratitude, apparently because
that amiable personage didn’t haughtily order a flunkey
to remove the visitant with a pair of parlour-tongs.
Female book-canvassers, who manage to get an audience
on the strength of a subdued manner and widow’s weeds,
come away in ecstacies and tumble over a female deputa-
tion coming in with vague ideas of what their grievance
really is, being only clear on the point that if Her Ex. put
up with them for 20 minutes they will all solemnly nod to
her on every occasion that offers, and speak with authority
on vice-regal movements and Intentions for the rest of
their reign. I wonder if Lady J. has the remotest idea of
how many female friends she’s got ?
Talking about vice-regalities, Lady Onslow, accompanied
as far as Moss Vale by Lady Jersey, and squired for the
whole journey by Mr. Gosohen, left Sydney, (o catch the
Oroya at Adelaide* in the vice-regal state car on 3rd inst.
Lady O. is a young-looking personage, with dark hair and
eyes, and a face untouched by Art, and her travelling
dress of pale-grey tweed, with loose jacket open over a
white shirt and tie, topped off by a small white sailor hat
and veil, became her admirably. Her brace of infants
were there, too—“Huia” (a little podge of a child), stiffly
starched in white embroidery, and a little daughter in a
white sailor costume with blue collar and man-o’-war hat.
By the way, the vice-regal state-car is a cut above the
white carriage (like a frozen-milk truck) that bore Caring-
ton conspicuously to and fro. Jersey’s perambulating
shrine is in three divisions—the main idea being a
drawing-room, got up in strawberry - and - gold satin
brocade, with small tables and chairs in white cane, and
window-drapes in brocade and ruby plush. Off this
magnificence is a boudoir for ladies, upholstered (the
room, not the women) in pearl-grey satin brocade ; while
off the other end of the drawing-room, the gentlemen’s
smoking den, in brown leather, opens out. It must be a
gorgeous panorama to travel amongst, and the only pity
is that the arrangements don’t include limelight, to throw
on the very special gold-laced guard who always hangs on
the rear platform of the car as a guarantee of good faith.
“Salvation processions are now the great rage,” as
Fred. Leslie used to sing, and if they work much deeper
into the system of the “ mahses,” kerridge folk will have
to submit to coachee in flowing black trews and unpadded
weskit—judging by the recent experience of a Potts
Point lady. Her appeal for a Jehu had been answered by
a Saved Soul with a grovelling reference from his last
place, so he was joyously engaged on the spot and sent
off to the tailor’s for a brand-new livery—green and pink
■with the breeches a skin-tight fit in white doeskin. But
when the man of threads came to them, the Saved Soul
with a forbearing smile, told him “ not to trouble about
cutting of ’em out just at once like,” and beat a retreat
back to his situation and demanded to see the “ missus.”
“ The fak is, ma’am,” he said, bashfully, “ them breeches
are too tight for any man who believes in being clad
■decently, and I carn’t take the place if they goes with it„
ma’am—it’s no better nor a play-hactor !” He was dis-
missed indignantly, tor, of course, the matron couldn’t
afford to spoil the look of her box-seat with a pair of
reefed garments.
A most officially sacred Sydney lady is in despair just
now over a box of toys which she sent to some children
in England by a cruising relative just departed thence.
The toys were tiny green frogs, looking harmless enough
at sight, but when they were half way home the sender
■discovered—by a sample left behind—that each frog was
loaded with dynamite! Cables were sent to warn the
recipients of the Anarchistic nature of the toys, and now
The sacred lady afore-mentioned has horrible visions of
the whole of her small and unsuspecting relatives being-
blown sky-high and coming down in “ merry little bits”
an the next country.

A hard-up Southern journalist came home at 7 a.m. the
other day, and his wife began to make it brisk for him,
until at last, to pacify her, he said : “ Did I toll you I am
going to town to get some money to-day?" “ Judging
by the hour you came home, 1 should say you got some
yesterday,” was her telling reply. And yet they say
women cannot reason.

Uncers are to be tabu at the best balls next season
because the more unchained festivities such as the “hoc”,
man’shop” or the “bricklayer’s ball” take to such Terpsi-
chorean riots like ducks to water, and of course society
cannot model its dance-eaid on the “program” of the
two-and-sixpenny social. Which reminds me that the
committee of the Hobart private assemblies’ very smart
hops, to which even the droit d’entree. at Government
House doesn’t make \ou eligible for an invitation, have
notices hung up in the room “requesting that no kitchen
lancers he indulged in ” for the season ’92. Last year one
or two demure ladies who found themselves involved in
such things quietly left the set and sat down by their
chaperones. At the semi-public assembly such a marked
act of disapproval is quite “in order,” but in a private
house it would be a discourtesy to one’s hostess—there-
fore the news that botli the rough-and-tumble romps are
out of court for the coming Season will be hailed with
deep and pious joy by dancing girls who don’t care to be
all the evening pinning up ripped gathers.

In “Fifty Years of Colonial Life,” Mr.
Sizar Elliott, of Melbourne, relates a ghastly
experience of the unreliability of circumstantial
evidence. Away back in the Forties, when he
was in Sydney, he was a member of a jury that
tried a case of murder heard before Chief Justice
Forbes. Near Campbelltown (N.S.W.), one
evening, two men, both riding on one horse, were
returning home from work. They were heard
quarrelling as they rode along the track. In the
morning, the front man was found dead on the
road. His mate declared that he had been shot.
There was under the right arm a wound which
the post-mortem medico declared was just such a
wound as a gardener’s knife would make, and the
living man was a gardener. Chief Justice Forbes,
in summing up, told the jury the old story that
circumstantial evidence was just as good as, if not
better than, direct evidence. The accused was
found guilty and hanged. Twelve months later a
bushranger named Curran was condemned to be
hanged and made a dying confession. In that con-
fession, Curran owned up to the murder of the man
on account of whose death this gardener had been
hanged. He had- potted the front man on the
horse from behind cover. The remains of the
murdered man were exhumed, and the bullet
found in them ! The post-mortem doctor had only
made a perfunctory examination, and had not
known by sight enough to distinguish between a
ball puncture and an incised wound! Says Mr.
Elliott: —
Where was my heart, think you . . . when I found
that I had.been a consenting party to an innocent man’s
death? . . . I vowed never to believe circumstantial
evidence again. Many years afterwards, on a trial at our
Court in Melbourne for murder, the counsel, on opening,
stated the evidence to be adduced was solely circumstan-
tial. I jumped up and told the Judge 1 would not convict
under these circumstances. He patiently listened to me,
and then said, in a determined and stately manner, “ Sit
down, sir, and listen to the evidence ; you have been
sworn.” Fortunately there was no case and I was
relieved. I still think 1 would not have consented to
a second death ; the shock of the first was enough for
a lifetime.

Sydney Criterion continues to hold the
mirror up to farcical improbability, and to fill the
house. On Saturday last, Robert Brough, of
legal reputation, dunned the mantle of Haroun
al Raschid, and perambulated the streets as a
nineteenth-century caliph in search of derelict
adventure. After befriending a loud - slurted
female acrobat, with a sore throat, and a per-
sistently locatory mania, he subsided beneath
the roof-treo of his step-mother with a
female too many in his family connections,
and misery ever afterwards. It is now nearly
four years since we last gloated over this
specimen of joy and jugglery, but, to the shame
of our cynicism, it has lost nought of its frivol-
someness. Brough adds an easy perfume to the
violet, and forces one to forget St. Maur of other
days with a dulcet facility befitting a slippery
skating-rink. The new lady, Miss Ida Liston, ia
not altogether Maggie Knight. She does, may-
hap, sing George Lash Gordon’s topical song with
a deal more esprit; but she certainly lacks that
pyrotechnic splendour that enabled the erstwhile
“ gutta-percha girl ” to strike a lucifer on the sole
of a dainty shoe. Cannam, albeit good, is not
“Teddy ” Kqyee, and one misses the unction of a
voice in which humour rolled like “coming”
butter over a churn-flap. Miss Brenda Gibson is
an admirable American niece, who makes us
regret that we have no nieces of that nationality
ourself. Mr. Fabert is a sly-faced and fat
voiced Dobson, reminiscent of many purloined
dishes, and Miss Romer, consummate artiste
as she is, is the only lady on the stage in'
Australia, or anywhere else", capable of being
Hummingtop’s mamma. Likewise Miss Major’s
Barbara, is a servant-girl whom one may make
several ineffective efforts to avoid tipping,
at * * *
The Coogee Aquarium has added a human
feature to the attractions of the deep, and stalwart-
bipeds meander in vigorous contest without the
marble basin of the show. The Bondi Aquarium
is also open. So likewise is the Polytechnic,
beneath a new management, with a seductive
exhibition of fleas—chained. Other more or less
aggressive places of amusement continue to clang
the tocsin of a whilom delightfulness, and the
fetching Albus sing every Sunday.

“Caste,” at the Bijou, proved such a
-weet restorer of jaded business that the manage-
ment decided to give Anson another week in which
t 0 S ay farewell. Consequently Eccles will get
drunk in the bosom of his family, and commit a
nightly burglary upon that stuck-up little beast,
the rag baby, until Friday. The baby is the one
item that doesn’t advance nearer to perfection in
the B. and B. rendering of “Caste.” It never
seems to cut a tooth or try to feel its feet, and a
sorrowful conviction that the wretched impostor
was born without feet, or a face to call its own, is
growing upon an otherwise admiring public.
However, baby must leave the cradle forthwith,
and “Captain Swift,” with Tith. in the name-
part, will be duly produced on Saturday.

“Little Jack Sheppard ” humps his
“bluey” and departs from the Royal about the
same time. The gay marauders have hugged
Bess and Poll with fair success for three weeks,
and the world yearns, as per advt., to see the
winsome Billee Barlow in one more character
before her engagement expires. For which reason
“Randolph the Reckless” is likely to pop up on
Saturday, if he can be brought to the scratch in a
presentable condition by that date. The Ran-
dolph under discussion was originally intended to
be Churchill, but by any other name he is certain
to be reckless. All Randolphs are built that way.
** * »
Melbourne Opera House has been freely,
if not lavishly, attended of late, and Carl Hertz
seems to keep the ball rolling better than any
other conjuror who has visited these parts. On
Saturday he struck out in a new direction by
burning a lady alive and reproducing her from the
ashes, so evidently he will be a useful man for any
sinner to know in the great hereafter. The
Graggs are likewise getting further and further
past all understanding with their wonderful legs,
but the day of their departure is nigh. They are
under London engagement to Sir ’Gustus Harris,
a bloated aristocrat who has commanded them
to leave Australia by the next Orient boat, and as
there is no arguing with a proud knight the
Graggs must go. Consequently the Empire Com-
pany will have to be wound up for the present by
about Thursday of next week, leaving the stage
clear for Mrs. Bernard-Beere and her party from
England. The lady will appear first “As In a
Looking Glass,” and uncommonly nice she will
look.
< *
Meanwhile, “La Cigale ” has a fight hold
at the Princess’. The ladies’ orchestra, which was
soothing (when the darlings didn’t play), but very
superfluous, has been dispensed with. Generally
speaking, the first violins suffered from bad head-
aches, and the wind instruments were dreadfully
upset about something George had been saying,
whilst the delusive make-weight damsels, who
really didn’t produce any music at all, were con-
stantly coming over faint and threatening to drop.
In fact, manager Williamson found the ladies’
orchestra such a strain on his constitution that
he is supposed to have ended the matter
by paying them to go into the next street.
The moral of “La Cigale” is still shrouded in
mystery. Flora Graupner, for instance, is alleged
to personify the industrious ant, but seeing that
she wears a white satin dress and occupies her
time with dodging round for a chance to sing, they
might just as well call her an earwig. The low
comedians, Elton and Lauri, are also difficult to
reconcile with any particular meaning, but they
have a good deal to say and are ably supported by
a white donkey, who bears the burden of their
long yam in the market-place without kicking.
** * »
Melbourne theatres, by the way, are feeling
the effects of Exhibition Concerts every Saturday
night. The three halls are still locked in a death-
struggle, each claiming to be wiping-out the other
two. At the Waxworks, they have got the clever
Japanese troupe, lately with Frank Clark, whilst
the Aquarium is relying to a great extent upon
its raging alligator, and the Cycloramas are
Progressing as usual.

Billy Swords, the Melbourne amateur, was acting Sol-
dering Solomon, the raiser tinker, in “ Rory O'More,”
and emerged from his cave hugging his “ Go-o-ld !
go-o-ld!” in a handkerchief, but it burst and scattered old
bits of china all over the stage—after all, the profoundest
millionaire moral.
Real lightning is introduced on the German stage, and
answers so well that it will soon be universal. Yet they
don’t come up to Lola Montes, at Bendigo, according to
the yarn, acting in “Asmodeus, or the Little Devil,”
when a thunderstorm burst overhead and lightning
shivered a plank of the stage. Lola coolly remarked to the
manager, “ Your effects in this piece are very creditable.”
Perhaps Anson’s Eccles is the best ever seen. George
Honey, the original, gave a tinge of burlesque to every-
thing, but he actually bought out a tap loafer for Eccles’
clothes, even as one of our Australian actors stripped an
actual navvy for Hawkshaw the Detective, “ Shall I
wash them?” innocently asked his wife.

supposed to be Mrs, Shaw, the handsome whistling
woman.
A Melbourne bookseller tumbles out on his table the
whole theatrical library bought from Mrs. G. B. \V.
Lewis (Rose Edouin.) Materials for his histrionic history
are here galore, with marked prompt-books of the Lewis
managements, in Calcutta, Bombay, and Melbourne.
There are even the Byronio burlesques, in which Rose
Edouin acted, as a girl, at Coppin’s little wooden
Pantheon Theatre, Oremorne, Melb., over 30 years ago—
but, then, look at Farren ! Under-study to Ninon de
I’Enclos.
Charles Warner, in the Xmas number of The Theatre,
exhumes the narrative of Federici’s death descending the
trap as Mephistopheles, at Melbourne Princess’s. The
regular theatrical death to trot out in these reminiscences
is that of John Palmer,who fell dead on the stage, while
acting the “Stranger,” but it was not at the words “There
is another and better world,” which are, of course, always
picked out of the play, as the most apt. Fullon died on
the Dublin stage, just walking off at the wing, as Sir
Peter Teazle.
Mrs. Bernard Beere’s complimentary benefit at London
Haymarket netted £4OO. Of the gentlemen who accom-
pany her, says The Bulletin's correspondent, you will
be sure to like Herbert Standing, who, in addition to
being a sound actor, is a real good all-round man, who
can take his own part at anything. He is a pretty rough
’un with the mittens. He is a brother of Frank Celli,
the well-known baritone. It is to be hoped that Marius
will receive a welcome, for he is a merry and amusing
fellow. It is generally admitted that he was treated
badly by Florence St John, who wanted to get rid of him
at any cost. It was not his fault that the affair came into
court.
When Johnny Solomon thought of a name for his
Sydney theatre, years ago, he selected “Bijou.” One
day a Semitic friend asked him what was to be the name
of the house. “ The Bee Jew,” replied Johnny. “Don’t,
Solomon, too many call us ‘B —— Jews’ already 7, and
this is only encouraging ’em. Call it something else.”
And sure enough, Johnny did—he called it the
“ Criterion.”
Theatrical management, says the gallant Coppin, would
be very pleasant if all “stars” we® like Billee Barlow.
Yet Miss Billee is not a radiant star when you come to
consider her seriously. She sings a comic song distinctly
in a fairly musical voice, she dances very little and very
seldom, she speaks her rhymed fines quite mechanically,
not to say monotonously, when there are several to be
spoken at a stretch. Billee’s strong points are her fine
appearance, music-hall chic, and infectious air of festivity
and good humour.
Cecil Ward is about to present himself in England “ on
his marriage.”
The Bernard-Beere is a large-framed lady of the type
that Burne Jones used to paint. Her gestures are angular
and Brown-Potterish, but her brains are all there.
H. A. Jones, who is running his own plays at his own
theatre, selected Vanderfelt, a comparatively unknown
actor, to play the hero in a revival of “Judah.”
Ephraim Sells says he will probably quit the circus
business very soon. He is getting old, and has made a
fortune.
John Sullivan, son of Barry Sullivan, is with Miss
Beddard’s Company, which opened at Adelaide Royal.
John is no great shucks as an actor, and has a lot to
learn.
One of Sells’ elephants caused a consternation when
the show was leaving Adelaide. The Jumbo wanted to
eat a piestall, proprietor included, but the latter dis-
appeared, leaving the pies to their fate. The elephant
tasted one—and left.
Sells Brothers say they had the biggest houses of their
tour in Adelaide, and the Adelaide profits averaged
better than anywhere. The partners are very wroth
about the Sydney authorities quarantining their horses.
They say the horses were in good order, and that they
have since been re-shipped to America, under a clean bill
of health.
Melbourne Royal management has, it appears, lost
£5741 in the past six months, £4OOO of the debit being
due to Laura Villiers and her gruesome failures. Only
the pantomime and “Jack Sheppard” have shown any
profit since the restored theatre started business. In
other words the house never began to prosper until the
Age withdrew its “ support.”
Marie Halton, putting little pleasantries on one side,
changed her name and estate not so very long since.
Sells’ Circus, in its progress through Victoria, is buying
up all kinds of Australian curiosities, for the Chicago
Exhibition, and a white kangaroo is particularly wanted.
Says Coppin: “ A brother manager tells me he has
serious thoughts of shutting-up for 12 months.”
Why can’t the Italian Operatists give us Mascagni’s
“ Oavalleria Rusticana ?” They are so infernally lazy,
and yell out “Moro Studio!” on the slightest pretext,
but here is the opera score to hand, in every music shop.
Dampier puts up the “Wandering Jew” again, in
Melbourne, with his Dagobert. When he first came to
Melbourne, Rose and Lily were infantile, but he went
away for some years, and reappeared with them as
the two children in the “Wandering Jew, or All For
Gold.” The public was, of course, much interested.

It’s hard to tell this yarn of the celebrated John
Edwards the Younger, in Melbourne. An English pro-
vincial actor, ofhumble rank, used to be very frowsy in
his ways, but went to America and made a rise. He came
back full of cash and says to his old manager, “ Cully ! I’ll
star a night or two, but won’t take anything, because I’m
lozy with money.” “Yes,” replied the boss, “and I
knew you when you were lozy without money.”

Opera House scenes, warning that the stage must be kept

Was it Lord Salisbury? George Honey, the original
Eccles, was not one of the snobby comedians, like .
He presided at a social club, where a member was a noble
lord, who had lately emerged from the chrysalis of
literary Bohemianism into millionairism. A gorgeous
flunkey stepped into the club to inform his lordship that
the carriage waited, but Honey, in the chair, severely
reprimanded the nobleman, who humbly apologised.
Shocked at the success of the Sunday concerts—
“admission free, collection at the door’’—the Sydney
clergy are now engineering underground to have them
stopped.
One hundred pounds was the price paid to Jude for his
song “ A Hundred Fathoms Deep ” —a pound a fathom—
and it was written in a night.
Organist Jude is evidently “struck” with Tasmania,
his departure for Maoriland having been announced some
weeks before his second “farewell” performance at
Hobart. He has given several “farewells” since then,
fetching larger audiences each successive time.
Charles Warner, in a theatrical paper, narrates a curious
thing. Graoie made her first stage appearance, as Juliet
to his Romeo, balcony scene, at his farewell benefit,
Drury Lane, leaving for Australia. A wealthy gentleman,
attending the show, got mashed on her, but an adven-
turess persuaded him that she herself acted the part,
being Warner’s sister, and Graoie did not go on, through
stage fright. She showed him all her faded wreaths,
whereupon he married her, and has now gone to the
asylum. Warner has no sister, but has a brother, and a
strapping son.

Bertram, the conjurer, was doing some card tricks, one
night recently, at the Savage Club (London), when
Christie Murray rose up with a look of cold derision in
his eye and wanted to show the audience “ how it was
done.” There was such an air of lofty disdain about him
that the conjurer got mad and “ went for him.” Before
Murray could get fairly started with his exposure, cards
began to fall out of him in all directions—down the legs
of his pants, off the top of his head, down his cuffs—
everywhere. Then the conjurer (who was sitting at the
other end of the room) proposed that Murray and Sandow
(the strong man) should divide a pack of cards between
them. They did it, and Bertram waved his hands in the
air, and ten of Murray’s cards vanished right there, and
turned up in Sandow’s pocket. Then more cards began
falling out of Murray’s clothes, and the dogmatic author
retired in disgust, without exposing anybody. He was
probably afraid if he held his ground much longer that
his clothes would fly off him and he would be left standing
up in the middle of the room a naked novelist.
