by the Rev. John Robertson, in the Free Gorbals’
Church, Glasgow, Scotland :
The Lord make this bereavement (Clarence’s death) a
thinking time for him (Wales), and may he stop his horse-
racing and all going to doubtful places, &c., &c.
Good old extempore!
W. H. Campbell, the defeated squattophile
candidate for the Barcoo, W.Q., tried to ensure
his return by promising loudly that he would
advocate the unconditional release of the unionists
now incarcerated in St. Helena penal establish-
ment for alleged rioting and conspiracy. But the
Labour voters were not to be “had” that way,
and secured his rejection by 200 votes. The
victory of the shearers’ party is a good omen for
the coming great struggle.
The story of the Larkin-Cleary jury.
When the jury retired, six were for a conviction,
five for an acquittal, and one for a disagreement.
One juror said—“ Look here, Mat Larkin did me
a good turn. He flung his money about like a
prince, he did ; and I’ll be (asterisked) if I’ll pot
him—(dash-blank) the (thisanthat) evidence.’
Three jurymen held that a cheque which Larkin
received, as part of land proceeds, was the return
of a loan. Reappearing before the Judge, that
theory was exploded. Next, the acquittal men
took the ground that Cleary was only a tool of
Larkin, and that therefore there could be no
“conspiracy” (of which precise offence the
prisoners were accused). Had there been an
acquittal, a detective stood ready in court to arrest
the two men again on fresh and separate charges.
So the jury disagreed.
Sowclen, chief of the S.A. Register
reporting-staff, takes up F. W. Ward’s vacated
position in London as cablegrammer to the
Associated Australian newspapers. Each paper
contributing to the cable-fund nominates a man in
turn. First the Age nominated Phil. Mennell;
then Sydney D.T. nominated F. W. Ward ; now
it is Adelaide’s turn. The post is aby no means
desirable one, and gives a man the life of a dog.
. Its holder has to arise every day before three a.m.,
at which time cabling begins ; any messages not
in before 5.30 reach Australia too late for that
day’s publication. Then one has three masters to
please in three different provinces, and each snarls
fiercely unless his own province is given special
prominence in the news, to obtain which all sorts
of expedients have to be resorted to. By the way,
the present Melbourne attempt at newspaper
coalition is founded to no small extent on a deter-
mination on the part of the leading newspaper-
proprietors to get a cable-monopoly and squelch
all competition.

Should a prisoner, tendering his evidence
under the new law permitting the examination on
oath of arraigned persons, be allowed to enter the
witness-box, or should he be sworn in the dock?
Judge Docker (N.S.W.) says that no prisoner
should be allowed to leave the dock. Judges
Backhouse and Coffey, on the other hand, treat an
accused man as an ordinary witness. They are
right. Every man is legally innocent until proved
guilty. If you are going to sw T ear a man behind
the spikes, why should you not allow the Crown
Prosecutor to open proceedings by tendering to
the jury a list of his previous convictions? Any
witness compelled to give evidence from a
prisoner’s dock—the symbol to an ordinary jury-
man’s mind of crime and depravity—is almost
hopelessly handicapped. Every accused man is
entitled to a fair trial, and, in view of that prin-
ciple, one cannot help asking why a man should
be caged like a wild beast until after he has been
convicted.
Melbourne Age professes to discover that
the Victorian Civil Service is overmanned by just
3077 servants ; lays down the law that, as officers
retire by effluxion of time, all new appointments
“must absolutely cease” until the service is
reduced to a maximum of 10,000 persons; says
that .£20,000 a year can be saved in the Govern-
ment Printing Office, another £20,000 a year by
abolishing the Public Service Board with a view
to having its duties performed by the Audit Com-
missioners (as originally proposed by Mr. Service),
estimates a possible saving in the railway depart-
ment by reduction of train mileage and working
expenses, at £500,000, and finds a total of
£911,000 per annum which it is within the power
of a resolute Government to “ retrench,” without
inflicting injustice either on the Civil Service or on
the public.

vincial grog-bill to £5,000,000, and (calculating on

the Treasury without extra taxation.

The “.Death Watch.”
A HOUSEHOLD FEST WHICH MAKES,
UNCANNY NOISES.
\\ ho has not heard the “ death watch ?”■ Many
a person who has happened to be awake- in the--
rtillness of the night has listened to the tick, tick :
tor half a dozen times or more in succession as •
regular and as natural as the ticking of a clock..
Many have regarded the sound with superstitious s
awe and dread, because everything about it is -son
mysterious. The name death, watch is derived inn
this way, as a person sitting up with the sick isis
more apt to hear such sounds, and when thfeyy
were heard at such times,, it was considered an ilkl-
omen to the sick one.. Some intrepid persona
dared to brave this messenger of,death, and found,!
instead of the supposed spirit* a guileless*.timid!
little insect. However, the name is still.- retained!,
and no doubt many still wonder what makes that
peculiar sound and why it is made. It is wonder-
ful that the mere tick, tick, tick of this harmless
insect should cause such fear and dread m some
people’s minds, who are generally so apathetic in
the matter of the serious signs of approaching
catastrophe, that spitting of phlegm in the morn-
ing, dull aching feeling in the head, eyes, and
back, languor, all tell of the increasing debility of
the system. Hecourse must be had to a genuine
restorative which will build up the general strength
and restore the normal health as in the case of
Miss M. Wignall, 4(> Fen wick-street, North
Carlton, Melbourne, who writes: “During the
last seven years 1 have suffered dreadfully irom
neuralgia, passing many sleepless nights of agony
in consequence. So much had the pain interfered
with my general health that I had wasted away
to a shadow, many of my friends thinking I was
consumptive. I tried all kinds of remedies with-
out success until I used Clements Tonic, of which
the first bottle gave me instant relief, and I feel
that Clements 1 onic is thoroughly rt invigorating
my whole system, giving me a good appetite and
relish for food, and I have therefore much pleasure
in endorsing the merits of Clements Tonic.

Under an old statute, a princess, betrothed
to an English prince in direct line of succession to
the throne, in the event of her affianced husband
dying, is incapacitated from contracting another
marriage within five years after his demise.

When the late Sir John Hay was first
elected for the Murrumbidgee, he sent a circular
to every elector whose name appeared on the roll,
making enquiries as to local wants. One man of
the large number that replied asked for “a bridge
across the creek to hotel, cis it is.too far to be
walking five miles round to the ferry whenever
you want a drink.”
Apropos the Ninian Melville - Toohey
quarrel in N.S.W. Assembly. When the former
was an auctioneer and was about to hold a small
suburban sale “ the talent ” amongst the dealers
began to chaff, whereupon Melville declared he
would have no noise and sent for a constable to
clear the room. Imagine an auction-sale without
chaff, and minus an audience ! Ninian is a
teetotaller. “No teetotaller can be a successful
auctioneer,” says Walter Bradley.
R. L. Stevenson, the novelist, is still at
Samoa. Things are looking more unhealthy
every year in the South Sea Islands, and if he
stays there long enough his last novel—the one he
writes just before his funeral—will have a huge
sale. What a rush there would be for a book by
one of the most popular writers alive, with his
portrait—in the last stages of leprosy—opposite
the title-page, and a preface stating how, his
manuscript was disinfected before going to the
printer ! If Stevenson doesn’t want to see a most
unhealthy demand for that final work he had
better clear out.
When the late District Judge Callaghan
came to N.S. Wales as a young barrister, he used
to attend the Quarter Sessions held in the Southern
District. On one occasion he had a brief to defend
a man charged with horse-stealing, then a very
prevalent offence. The case against accused was
very weak, and Crown Prosecutor Cheeke intended
to enter a nolle prosequi. Hearing jof this, Cal-
laghan went to him and asked him to put the man
on his trial, pointing out that it would give him a
great lift if his first sessions case should result in
an acquittal. The case being so doubtful, and
the man being defended, and therefore (as he
thought) not likely to suffer, Cheeke consented.
He was accordingly tried and, despite poor Tom’s
eloquence, was convicted. This was a joke against
Callaghan until his dying day.
“Arabi, the Egyptian,” when he went
into captivity in Ceylon at the behest of England
and her usurers, was a tab, well-proportioned,
erect man of soldierly bearing, with but the
merest shimmer of frost on his short, strong beard.
Now, after eight years of exile, he is round-
shouldered and flabby, saturated with malaria, his
limbs rheumatically crippled, his beard and hair
white. At the age of 51, he presents the appear-
ance of a man of 70. Asked why he didn't go
into the hills, he excused himself on the score of
poverty. He is allowed .£5O a month, paid by
Egypt, out of which .£25 a month is deducted for
the maintenance of Ins wife and family in Egypt;
then his eldest son, also in Ceylon, takes .£l2 10s.
a month, and Arabi lives on the balance also
.£l2 10s. a month. He states that he is unable to
give his children the education they ought to
receive. Lord Dufferin originally certified to
<£2ooo a year on Ai'abi’s account, but tire allow-
ance was “ retrenched ” to £OOO.

when the work is started. The worst of it is that
it has been decided to engage labourers in Mel-
bourne, so that these men will be left out in the
cold. “Move on!”
The Young Victorian Patriotic League is
a sucking Tory attempt to incorporate “ patriot-
ism ” with proputty. The league pipes forth, in
its childish treble, a list of the principles and
institutions that every citizen must respect, and
if he thinks the principles are narrow and the
institutions rotten, then he can’t be a patriot.
Nobody objects seriously to the league claiming
the monopoly of a large word any more than they
felt offended' when Parkes moved the resolution
that N.S.W. was Australia. If stockbroker
Mendell and his friends called themselves the
Young Victorian Idiotic League, it wouldn’t
follow that the idiots in the asylums were not
entitled to their old name.

Dear Bulletin,—On December 4th or sth
last some drovers found the body of a man stuck
in the fork of a tree in the Boyan River, a few
miles below Gongolgon, at a place called The
Pink Hills. There was a deep wound in the fore-
head, and the skull was broken in. People in the
vicinity have often wondered if an enquiry was
held, and, if so, the decision arrived at by the
officiating J.P. or Coroner. One thing is plainly
evident—the drovers who found the body have
never been called upon to testify in the matter.
Simpson, accountant of the South Mel-
bourne Building Society, told the usual building
society story when he got into the witness-box to
testify against that fallen saint, Matthias Larkin.
Loans were granted, he said, in a loose and
general sort of way by the manager or the secre-
tary, or anybody, and sometimes the directors
were consulted, and quite as often they were not.
Sometimes there was security and sometimes there
was none ; a lot of the alleged debts duo to the
society were entirely bogus, and though the
auditors drew their fees regularly, and handed in
their certificate that all was correct, there had
been no audit for years. As far as concerned the
shareholders’ interests the society might, it seemed,
as well have been managed by an octopus and
audited by a corpse.
A movement is afoot among certain
Sydney newspaper proprietors and master-printers
to effect a reduction of the current wages of
compositors. A meeting of typos, was held the
other night, convened by a “rat” printer who is
said to be a past-master in such negotiations, and
the result is boastingly stated to have been that
between 50 and GO comps, have expressed their
readiness to accept, as a “stab” rate of wages,
£2 15s. per week —a clear reduction, on “stab,” of
ss. per week —and something like £'2 a week
less than can be regularly earned, “ by the thous-
and,” by a competent frame-holder on any daily.
The proprietary of the Australian Star we hear,
has declined to listen to the proposals of the “ra t ’
negotiator, and has expressed its complete satis-
faction with existing rates.
It is recorded, on absolutely unimpeach-
able authority, that during the great French
Revolution and the Reign of Terror which
followed it, when the king and queen were
executed, and heads fell like tiles, and nine
nations were making simultaneous war upon
France, that there were a few peaceful old fossil
citizens who lived on in the remote parts of the
Parisian suburbs, and didn’t know there was any
revolution at all. These quiet, clam-like in-
habitants pottered along in their gardens, and
grew their vegetables, and only learned by acci-
dent, when it was all over, that the greatest row
in the world’s history had been going on all round
them, and they hadn’t noticed it. After that it
seems quite credible that there should be various
aged inhabitants in N.S. Wales who never heard
even of Parkes, We met one of them once. He
was a placid old farmer near Bankstown, and he
livid by a primeval system of barter, swopping
His hay with a man at Parramatta for provisions.
He didn’t go in with it himself, being averse to
the madding crowd, but his son acted as Lis
agent. The old man didn’t much like the
arrangement, being under the impression that the
fortnightly visit to Parramatta would make the
youth a wild, dissipated man of fashion, but he
wouldn’t face the job himself, and either
the son had to go or the family must
have lived on grass. The old man’s ob-
jection to visiting the town appeared to
be that “there was too many lawyers there;’
evidently he imagined that a lawyer might seize
him at any time, and strip him naked on the
pavement, and his only hope was to keep out of
sight. He had a vague, shadowy idea that a new
bridge had been put up five years previously on
the main road to Sydney just two miles from his
lair, but he had never been along to see it. And
he had actually no recollections of Parkes. He
thought over him a long time, and didn’t seem to
get any nearer solving the mystery. But at last a
dull sort of light appeared in his eye—the sort of
light that you see when the new moon shines on a
dead fish—and he hazarded the theory that per-
haps he had something to do with Nicholls and
Lester and the old-time murders up the Parra-
matta River. The story of the placid Australian
farmer ends here, for that great thought exhausted
him, and he deposited himself in a shady spot
under the hedge to wait for dinner. And the
writer climbed on to his horse, and came away
wondering how much one man could contrive not
to know provided he only tried hard enough.

The Australian patent rights of an Ameri-
can, invention for the expeditious washing of
dishes, which in California alone realised a
profit of 74,000 dollars, was bought by two smart
Yankees for .£4OOO, and the pair hied them o’er
the (long wash of Australasian seas certain of here
making a speedy fortune. But they had been
measuring the Australian housefather by his
American prototype. The Melican man has a
I loupe full of little “notions” designed to lessen
the. work of his women folk, whereas the Aus-
tralian ditto seemingly loves to make his female
dependants “graft” all the time, so the Yanks
have had to sell out the invention here for as many
hundreds as it cost them thousands, and have
sadly departed.
* ■ * *
A Sydney man (which his name is Hill)
has just made <£sooo out of a patent sanitarv
appliance. He didn’t invent it, or steal it, or buy
it; he simply got it as a present from a friend in
America, who had a hazy idea that Australia was
about as large and important as Wyoming, and
who had no idea that he was giving away a tidy
fortune.
» * •
It is related that the man who persuaded
a certain Australian clergyman to fly to ’Frisco,
attended him to the steamer and" then went
straight to a daily newspaper office, and “gave
away the whole show.” A citizen who liked the
erring parson and knew the circumstances met
this genial joker aforesaid in the street a few days
later: “Excuse me, sir,” said he, with a genial
smile, “ but have I not the honour of addressing
Mr. Blank?” “Yes, sir,” said the informer,
proudly, “ that’s my name.” “It ought to have
been Judas,” replied the citizen, drily.

Forrest, Griffith and Playford—three Im-
perialists are the only Australian Premiers
who figure in the pages of “ Burke’s Colonial
Gentry.” Minister W. J. Lyne (N.S.W.) is
perhaps the only ex-council clerk in Australia
who believes he is a member of the “colonial
gentry.” He is the head of “ the Lynes of Gala.”
“Cupid” Smith, of Exhibition celebrity, was
by his innate modesty from posting
Burke up in his social and political achievements.
The story of the rise of the family of Smith, of
Castlesmith, will not be complete unless it tells of
the kindly manner in which its head was wel-
comed into the legal fold by Jndge Dickenson.
George Leith-Hay, son of a former Police-
Magistrate at Rockhampton and Ipswich (Q.), is
one of the secretaries in the Scotch department,
London. He is heir to estates in Scotland now
held by his uncle, and worth at least <£15,000 a
year. Besides this, the old gentleman has accumu-
lations which will tot up into six figures, so
George Leith-Hay is an eligible parti much sought
after by the British matron. Leith-Hay, pere,
was one of the most popular men in the Moreton
Bay district. One of his daughters married Lord
Henry Phipps, and his second son is connected
with an old firm of London wine-merchants—
Denman and Get, Piccadilly.

There are a good many quondam Austra-
lians in Cheltenham, Eng. Old “ Tom” Herbert,
one of the oldest Queensland pioneers, after whom
the Northern river is named : Tom Romo, who
ranks as “ a county gentleman Sir Charles
Stirling, an old Victorian ; John Brewster, whom
everybody in Sydney will remember, and who
divides his time between gardening and whist.
Then there are old Judge Forbes, a very ancient
Victorian identity ; the M’Laughlins, former
Port Phillip squatters ; Donald, a Bananalander ;
Mrs. H. H. Osborne, of Illawarra; and, last but
not least, Rodney Riddell (now Sir Rodney,
Bart.), who is one of the finest whist-players in
Europe. Note for whom it may concern: Rodney
is still a bachelor, and true to his early love.

There was at one time in Sydney a noto-
rious gang of successful shop-lifters. Dicky
Driver was their standing legal adviser, and lie
was very fortunate—thanks to Dailey, whom he
always retained to defend them when “ nicked ”
in procuring their acquittal. At last they were
caught red-handed, and Dick was hard-pressed
for a good defence. Dailey was retained at a
large fee, but ere his brief was delivered he was
secured for the defence of the V—-’s, ricli
squatters, who were to be tried for cattle-stealing
up North. The fee in this case was 1000 guineas,
which was too big for even W.B.D. to decline, so
he intimated to Driver that he could not possibly
attend to the interests of his old clients. Driver
was in despair and at once went to Darlinghurst
to convey the sad intelligence to the head of tho
gang. “But it will be all right,” said Dick, “I
shall be able to get W , and ho is quite as
good.” “ W—— !” shrieked the champion thief—
“W defend me! Not much. I wouldn’t
give him bug-soup to plead for me.”
The latest London society sensation is the
publication of a pamphlet said to be by a rather
distinguished newspaper man, giving the whole
story of the supposed liaison between the Prince
of and Lady , and a certain Lord (a
friend of the Prince) and the same lady. It is all
circumstantially told, and is generally admitted to
be true. The pamphlets were all type-written,
and about fifty of them were distributed. These
have all been recovered by the lady and her
friends, except three copies, one of which is in the
possession of Lady G. C., to whom the whole
thing is, of course, “nuts.” Hundreds of people
have read, or heard the pamphlet read, as the
latest London vogue has been to give afternoon
teas at which the lady of the house read the docu-
ment for the benefit of her guests. The supposed
author invites a prosecution, but they apparently
dare not attempt it.
The comparatively recent additions to tho
residence of a Southern bishop, seem to have
succeeded in “laying’’the ghost which a former
bishop and certain visiting parsons became quite
aceustomed to see roaming about the house and
precincts. The “ghost ” was popularly supposed
to be the shade of the wife of a former judge who
occupied the old house, whose convival habits not
only brought the family to beggary, but broke his
wife’s heart. She died some years after the judge,
in obscure poverty, while a son still lives, not a
hundred yards from the home of his childhood, in
a miserable shanty, eking out an existence by
selling milk.
* # *
The late Windsor (Melbourne) murder, in
which the victim’s be dy was bricked dow n and
cemented beneath a hearthstone, is something
like a murder, and recalls the good old days when
Mrs. Manning (who cooked her dinner in the
fireplace under which the had buried her victim)
made bhuk silk dresses unfashionable by choosing
to be hanged in one. A murderer who, with his
intended victim, appears suddenly like a stage-
sprite up a trap, vanishes mysteriously after
having sepulchred ids skull-battered sacrifice, and
gets two months’ start of the police, is not to be
sneezed at as a caterer for the evening press.
Murderers often forget something ; this one only
wanted a little quicklime for the hearthstone
secret to have been preserved for years. Till this
deed of gore, there has been hardly a recent
Australian murder that rose to tho altitude of
giving good value for the hanging ceremony.
trial for talking back from a pew to the Rev. Dr.
Hay, Presbyterian minister, in the pulpit.
“Should preachers be applauded?”

* * *
Dr. Scot - Skirving says that doctors
generally do two things “ neither wisely nor well ”
signing certificates as to health ; and appearing
as expert witnesses in courts. For the latter he
suggests a previous conference of medical wit-
nesses on both sides of the case in which they are
called. Ho, ho ! Why not a conference of and
anterior agreement between opposing lawyers?
That would be just as promising as a medical
caucus.

The face glove is the latest beauty device
in England. It is worn three days a week—the
face is put in curl-papers, as it were, and the
wearer is made beautiful for ever.
• # »
Labouchere tells a good story of a Bible-
class youngster. “ Who was Moses?” asked the
examiner. “The son of Pharoah’s daughter,”
was the quick reply of a little girl of 12. “No,
no,” said the questioner; “Pharaoh’s daughter it
was who found Moses among the bullrushes.”
“ Yes, ma’am,” promptly answered the child, “so
she said.”

Guy Labertouche, son of the popular
“P.P.,” Secretary of Victorian Railways, has
passed out of Sandhurst, and has been gazetted
to the Suffolk Regiment, better known as the old
12th, which served so long in Australia, and of
which Australia’s only General was for some time
Adjutant.
* ,* *
A Melbourne paper suggests that Queen
Victoria’s countenance should be removed from
the Australian sovereign and that its place should
be taken by a design showing a tropical sun
shining on a pound of butter. In view of recent
bank experiences this emblem is altogether too
slow to explain the way the money goes. A
pound of butter in Tophet would be nearer the
mark.

cutting down the shade-trees opposite the old
Australian Club (Sydney) premises induces one to
ask by whose authority the splendid fir-trees in
Bent-street, opposite the Education Offices, under
which was held the historical anti-transportation
meeting of 184!), were cut down ?

Sells’ Circus did great business at Geelong,
running up the extras with consummate art. As
the show concluded on a Saturday night there was’
a long peaceful Geelong Sunday to be got through,
and the circus bore it like a lamb. Local shep-
herds held services in the tent, at which the-
acrobats, clowns, Arabs, bearded ladies, etcetera,,
sang hymns with the grave decorum of an old
bachelor at a christening. Also a choice assort-
ment of Wesleyan maidens held a Bible-class;
IN SYDNEY IMPERIAL ARCADE.
The loafers stood around and jeered.
The painter-man took little note,
“ Salon of Photographic Art ”
Upon the window-pane he wrote.
1 saw no joke, and in the dark
I turned to one and craved a boon—
He answered: “ Mister, sttch a lark,
’Ee don’t know ’ow to spell ‘saloon.’ "
IN A MELBOURNE BAR.
She hath two eyes so soft and brown,
A dainty nose—
The goddess put her money down
On Wild Hose
(The winner for a sister s sake
She fancied thus):
Height) ! that sire our name would take
(From such a dream ’tis pain to wake)
And fancy us.
O ! Beer and backer (most absurd—
But yet a grip)
She thrilled us through with every word
That left her lip.
Our “ moral cert.” had never “ spun ”
Accursed moke !
Though we had lost and she had won,
We held her hand till ours had done
A lucky stroke.
ON THE BOUNDARY.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
I love that ancient boundary-fence,
That mould’ring chock and log.
When I go ride the boundary,
I let the old horse jog
And take his pleasure in and out
Where sandalwood grows dense,
And tender pines clasp hands across
The log that tops the fence.
’Tis pleasant, on the boundary-fence,
These sultry summer days ;
A mile away, outside the scrub,
The plain is all ablaze,
The sheep are panting on the camps,
The heat is so intense ;
But here the shade is cool and sweet
Along the boundary fence.
I love to loaf along the fence,
So does my collie dog,
He often finds a spotted cat
Hid in a hollow log ;
He’s very near as old as I
And ought to have more sense,
I ve hammered him so many times
Along the boundary fence.
My mother says that boundary-fence
Must surely be bewitched ;
The old man says that through that fence
The neighbours are enriched ;
It’s always down, and through the gaps
Our stock all get them hence,
It takes me half my time to watch
The doings of that fence.
But should you seek the reason
You won’t travel very far,
’Tis there a mile away among
The murmuring Belah :
The Jones' block joins on to ours,
And so, in consequence,
It’s part of Polly’s work to ride
Their side the boundary-fence.
BARCROFT H. BOAKE.

With unconscious humour, the London
D.T. commented on the quality of the people who
journeyed to Windsor to see the royal funeral
pageant. “Soon after mid-day the trains con-
taining visitors from London began to arrive. It
was a noticeable feature of these that most of the
■carriages were of the superior class, and that large
.—indeed, very large— numbers of their tenants
were ladies clad in the richest and deepest of
mourning.” The D.T. man seems to have been
surprised to find that there wasn’t a wild rush of
weeping dockers, and unemployed in sable tatters,
to attend the glorification of Duke Clarence’s
remains. Instead of which, the sorrowing pil-
grims consisted mainly of ladies, trimmed with
black velvet at 20s. a yard. Why, certainly !
Show us a woman, “high” or “ low ’’-born, in
rich trappings, and we’ll show you ten stone, or
thereabouts, of devotion to the Throne and
hysterical snobbery.
# # *
This is the story of two women who lived
in one street and hated each other like poison.
One woman had refused to marry the other
woman’s husband in the days when they both
were young, and the other woman knew it, and
loathed her because the husband had asked her
first. The other woman knew that the first
woman knew it, and loathed her in return.
Therefore when one of the husbands went away
for a few days on business the other one’s wife
wrote an anonymous letter to his grass widow,
stating that her partner had betr-r-rayed her, and
that if she called at a certain fast and disreputable
mansion in the suburbs she would find him there.
The recipient recognised the handwriting, and
though she believed the statements she loathed
the other female worse than ever for informing
her. She took a cab and went out in the dusk of
the evening to reconnoitre the house in ques-
tion. It grew dark while she was still hover-
ing about, and presently the gate swung
open and a well-known figure came out. Then
■all tier pent-up rage burst forth, and without
saying a single word she fetched the unsuspecting
man one with her umbrella. There came a shriek
of surprise; a well-known voice cried wildly for
the police ; and the assailant fled. She had made
a mistake. It wasn’t her husband at all; it was
the husband of her informant, who was also
“away on business.” When she got home she
telegraphed to her own partner, and found that
he was all right, and then, in the gladness of her
heart, she sat down and wrote another anonymous
letter, telling the enemy that her husband would
presently come home with a large bruise on his
head, received while coming out of another
woman’s house. And now these two ladies hate
each other even worse than they did before.

Edward Stephenson Smithurst (formerly
of Mudgee) lias declared himself insolvent. The
circumstances of his bankruptcy, brought about
by a rascally breach of faith, are such as entitle
him to the utmost sympathy of the public, and
certainly call for united action on the part of the
newspaper proprietors of N.S. Wales.

The run on the Mercantile Bank of Mel-
bourne was caused, according to the published
statement of a director, by tho fact that the
Speaker of the Assembly was chairman. And the
want of faith in another Melbourne bank, now
deceased, arose from the unfortunate circumstance
that Premier Munro was the moving spirit in it.
This influence of “representative gentlemen”
upon banks is very edifying.
# * #
The Thirteen Club—an association formed
with a view to knocking out a number of ancient
superstitions—recently held its annual dinner in
London. The guests walked solemnly underneath
a ladder, spilt the salt, crossed their knives and
forks, shook hands across the table, opened
umbrellas in the dining hall, and did other things
of evil omen and calculated to rouse up the powers
of misfortune. And the powers didn’t rouse
worth a straw. All the same the chances are that
the Thirteen Club is the most superstitious gang
in all London, and the members carry on like this
for the same reason that the boy in the church-
yard shakes his fiat at a bush on the off-chance
that it may be a hump-backed ghost. If one
member should chance to fall down dead at the
annual gorge it is more than probable that the
. club will get crushed to death in the doorway
while trying to escape, and none of them will ever
■ come back,

* * #
The Bulletin has just received a letter
from a man (no end of a swell, by the way) who
praises the N.S.W. hangman for having pulled up
'the cart he was driving so as not to run over a
valuable dog belonging to our correspondent. This
is about the mildest bit of praise yet brought under
our observation, but as we have not always spoken
kindly of J. Ketch we have much pleasure in
submitting the purport of the writer’s eulogy for
the consideration of our readers. We delete our
correspondent’s unmannerly suggestion that had
the cart been driven by a member of Parliament
the dog would probably have been slain.

Cabled that Sir H. Bessemer urges the
substitution of aluminium for paper for bank-
notes, to obviate forgery. He is a practical man,
and probably there will be an experiment witli
aluminium-leaf, which is very ductile, and only
one-fourth the weight of silver.

A picnic came off’ very successfully the
other day at Auckland (M.L.), barring one little
incident, which cast a temporary gloom over the
scene. There was a great social and religions
light present, and with him his little wife, aged
24. The good man was active in promoting the
festivities, and, among other things, lie got up a
race, open to “ all married women under 25.”
Being an economical person, he passed the hat
round until he collected a good prize, and then he
stood by smiling to watch his own little pansy-
blossom scoop the pool, and thus save him the
expense of now bonnets for six months at least
in fact, he had collected about enough to buy all
the pansy’s wardrobe for the ensuing half-year.
A WATERY EXCUSE.
BROKEN HILL BOY (who, prior to riding “ Buckjumper,” from which he has been thrown late-
high, had been sitting in a bucket of water to ensure a good seat) : “If I had some
more water to put on my trousers I’d git on him again.”
The race was regarded as a foregone conclusion,
but, then, women, horses and dogs are all un-
certain ; the blossom ran like a deer, but all she
could do was to make a dead heat with a hulking
female, who was not specially beautiful, and had
hair like a horse’s tail. Then the good man gob
enraged, and demanded that the hulking female
should be disqualified. Ho said she was over 25,
and she said that she wasn’t, and he said that he
wouldn’t believe her unless she produced her
certificate of birth. Thereupon, she said that she
would produce her certificate of birth if the other
lady would hand in her certificate of marriage,
and in a cold, hard voice she started to demand
that the pansy should be disqualified, because she
wasn’t a married woman at all, and somebody else
proposed to get up a handicap for ladies of doubt-
ful character and bigamists—but just here the
good man, with perspiration on his brow, suddenly
awarded her the prize, and stated in a husky voice
that the matter was too trivial to be worth dis-
cussing.
# # *
A pretty “steep” Gulf Country yarn.
Thus a correspondent :
The writer in 1889 went with others to a race-meeting
at , a telegraph station on the through line. The
races had been got up by the enterprising publican, and
the first event on the card was the Stockman’s Purse, for
all horses the property of 6 ona-jide stockmen, welter
weights. The course skirted along a low coolabah flat
and over the edge of a small sandy ridge, at the back of
which the horsemen were lost to view for a few seconds.
Nine started for the first race, and when they came into
view from behind the sandhill it was noticed that one
horse (a bald-faced chestnut) was among the others rider-
leas. The jockey had collided with a tree on the course
and was killed instantly right on the track. The crowd
merely ascertained that the man was dead, pulled the
body a few yards off the track, and proceeded with the
races. The corpse lay there all that day and part of the
next exposed to the myriads of flies, ants, &0., and the
blazing sun for over 30 hours, when two half-civihsed
blackfellows were sent by some of the man’s mates to
shovel him a few inches under the sand close to where he
fell.

Dear Bulletin, —Apropos your Wyndham
paragraph in a recent issue. On one occasion
Hugh Wyndham, the mainstay of whose religious
belief was the expectation of the immediate advent
of Christ, waited on W. H. Bond, of the Inverell
(N.S.W.) Times, for a price for printing a
pamphlet on his favourite subject. “ three
pounds,” said Bond. Mr. Wyndham demurred*’
whereupon Bond : “ Well, if the second coming
is so near at hand, what difference will a
couple of pounds make to you?” But Wynd-
ham didn’t see it in that light, and Bond lost the
job.—W.G.

# * *
A tumult of trumpeting was to be heard
outside Darlinghurst Courthouse, Sydney, the
other day. The noisy occasion was a “ gathering
of the elans” —a muster of the “Sydney High-
landers.” After half an-hour or so of hideous
screech and blare and fanfaronade, about 15 bare-
legged savages assembled. It is a healthful
sign that not more than 15 men could be
found wicked enough to parade the streets with-
out trousers. And they were all misorables with
poor turkey-legs.

* .. # *
A well-known Sydney insurance man the
other day visited an Eastern suburb to see a
friend, but was chagrined to find nobody at home.
As he stood at the gate pensively reflecting, two
smart Sydney detectives who knew him very
well, bade him good afternoon, and after an inter-
Chang* of civilities, one of the d’s., evidently
under the impression that the insurance man was
standing in front of his own premises, remarked,
respecting some web-feet visible inside the fence —
“ That’s a fine lot of ducks you’ve got.” “ Yes,”
said the lounger, “ they’re very fine ; but I’ve lots
more than you can see there. Perhaps you boys
would like a couple of pairs?” “ Well, you’re
very kind ; I don’t mind if I do,” said Detective
H. “ Much obliged,” added Detective R. “Well,”
said the joker, “ help yourselves. I’ve got to run
down the road to call for some ladies, so I can’t
wait. So long.” . . . The day after next, the
Sydney dailies each contained an advertisement
offering a reward for evidence as to the person
who stole four ducks, &c.

Is Bright’s Disease Curahle?
Physicians as a rule assert that Bright’s Disease
of the kidneys is incurable ; that while temporary
relief may be obtained, sooner or later the disease
will break out again with renewed virulence and
the victim at length succumb. Recently, when it
was stated that a high official in the United States
was afflicted with Bright’s Disease, a prominent
New York paper devoted considerable attention to
the subject, and in one of its issues published the
opinions of noted physicians in the Old World
which had been cabled to the newspaper.
It appeared to be the opinion of several medicos
that while Bright’s Disease is extremely dangerous,
it can be successfully treated. This is what
H. H. Warner and Co., proprietors of Warner’s
“ Safe ” Cure, have ever claimed, and it is some-
what gratifying to find that some doctors, at
least, do not pronounce a man’s doom and frighten
him to death as soon as it is known that he has
been attacked by Bright’s Disease. Warner’s
“Safe” Cure not only cures Bright’s Disease but
minor kidney difficulties as well ; for be it known
that Bright’s Disease is nothing more nor less
than disease of the kidneys in an advanced stage.
H. H. Warner and Co. will go a step further and
will guarantee that their medicine will cure
Bright’s Disease unless in its last stages, and when
the kidneys have not for the most part become
disintegrated and been voided with the urine.
No medicine can supply new tissues, but Warner’s
“Safe” Cure will check the waste and restore
what is left of the kidneys, to a healthy condition.
This is not a debatable question, for it has been
demonstrated that Warner’s “ Safe ” Cure has
cured thousands of cases of Bright’s Disease which
had been given up by the faculty, and best of all
the patients remain cured. Do not allow prejudice
to blind you to your best interests, but begin the
vise of this great kidney and liver remedy to-day,
if you have in your constitution any of the
symptoms that characterise this insidious foe to
human happiness and life.
Bright’s Disease is for the most part very
A insidious in its beginning. With few exceptions
the patient is unaware for the first few months or
even years that any grave kidney lesion is present.
( Do not neglect the first symptoms, viz., a nervous-
L ness or restlessness, weak and tired feelings in the
| legs, and a general sensation of exhaustion, with
Jtheadacho and loss of appetite, and general
depression of spirit-

SPORTING NOTIONS.
A truly pathetic sign of the times may be
noticed in the paddock at Flemington. Even the
leviathans of the ready-money ring give a cordial wel-
come to the modest “half jimmy” investment, whereas
three years ago they would crush the poor little punter
with the observation (floridly decorated) that they “didn’t
bet in ’arves."
• * *
The Australian custom of allowing five clear
days for a first-class cricket-match may seem desirable at
times, but it certainly reduces the chance of the game
being played out in three days.
# * *
Highborn’s victory reminds one again that
Carbine gave 3J stone to the black gelding in the Mel-
bourne Cup race of 189 X A few days before that High-
born had disappointed his party by knocking under to
Malvolio, who was receiving ten pounds. But Malvolio
turned out good enough to win the next Melbourne Cup.
What sort of horse was Carbine, and who will say that
Donald Wallace isn't justified in fixing his champion’s
Stud-fee at 200 guineas?
* # *
The Sydney proprietary clubs backed down
ignorniniously in their dispute with the bookmakers.
Why don’t they agitate for the totalisator ?
* * *
Although Bob Ramage and Hickenbotham
denied that there had been a quarrel, the former did not
have a mount on any of Wallaces horses during the
V.R.C. Autumn Meeting; and on the night before the
Newmarket a notice was in the Herald to the effect that
Bob was open to engagement.
* * *
Buyers of horseflesh who give a high price for
fillies of the Musket breed on the strength of the per-
formances of the colts almost invariably get “left.” As
a matter of fact, the best of the Musket mares have been
only moderates, and the winners are easily counted—
Necklace, Necklet, Hilda, Lady Norah, Industry, Volley,
and one or two others. Martini-Henri’s full sisters, Elfin
and Woodnymph, were rank duffers. Compare these
with the colts of that breed—Martini-Henri, Nordenfeldt,
Matchlock, Manton, Carbine, Maxim, Tirailleur, Trenton,
Strathmore, Zalinski—all amongst the best horses of
their time. The only nick where the fillies were better
than the fvals was the Musket—Locket, which produced
Necklace, the M.L. six-furlong record-holder, and Neck-
let, winner of the Maribyrnong Plate. Where the colts
are worth thousands, the fillies are not worth fifties.
Backers should paste this in their hats, and restrain a
d sire to back mares because they are sired by Norden-
feldt.
# # #
The art of race-riding, which has never been
widely cultivated in this country, seems in danger of
dying out altogether. Time was when one could name a
jockey, every here and there, who had “ followers,” and
whose mount “up” was considered worth backing for the
sake of the mm. But of all the jocks who used to win
races with their heads and hands, only Teddy Power is
left on the job, and he seldom figures in connection with
“good goods” nowadays. Hales and O’Brien stroll around
the scene of their former greatness in plain clothes, the
one looking not strong, and the other unmistakably weak.
Poor Alec Robertson is under the turf, whilst Gorry—a
veritable little nugget in his day—has faded into obscurity.
There are none to fill the places of these old favourites.
Racing has degenerated into a miserable exhibition of all-
round incapacity in the saddle, and although the boss
horses may seem to be judiciously ridden when they
have things all their own way they get precious
little assistance in the time of need, as witness
the lame finish of Strathmore when collared by Highborn.
Boys don’t ride at all, in the Archerian acceptation of the
term—they merely stick on their respective horses’ backs
and jostle round the track in a wild, wobbling mob.
Nearing “ the turn for home,” the few who carry instruc-
tions to win are apt to get hopelessly entangled with the
many who have been ordered to lose, and the “trier”
that suffers the least bad luck scores a “clever” victory
That is to say, he gets first past the post with his eyes
rolling from side to side and his brain whirling round.
He has lost his cap and his presence of mind, but has
managed to hang on to his whip. So execrable is the
horsemanship in the ordinary six-furlong flutter,
that the same race might be run six times with six
totally different results. The stewards, presumably,
are quite of this opinion, otherwise they would
call upon jockeys, trainers and owners to explain
the wholesale reversals of “form” which are killing
public interest in the short “whack ’em home”
events. The finish for the Bourke Handicap, last week,
was ludicrous to those who watched it in cold blood.
No sensible punter could pride himself on having picked
the winner out of such a swerving, sprawling struggle of
beaten horses, with flustered riders flourishing whips at
random. Four of the five first past the post had pre-
viously run unplaced in the Newmarket, whilst four of
the leading five in the Newmarket were badly left on this
occasion. The extra weight they carried (only 21bs. in
some instances) was not sufficient to account for this
turning of the tables, consequently the only honest
explanation of the puzzle as regards Wild Rose, Harbour
Light, Gladstone, and St. Blaize, is that they “got
through ” in the one scramble and were “ shut out ” of
the other. Punters at a distance will kindly accept this
intimation.

The cratty spider who has been pirating the
name and style of Tattersall Adams, and diverting sweep
funds into a private box rented by him at theO.P.O.,
was caught last Saturday in the act of posting 2000 of his
nice little printed illusions to Queensland. The
“demons,” for-convenience, charged him with having
attempted to circulate printed matter without an
imprint, and on this he is held to bail which is fixed at a
total of £BOO ! On the gentleman’s person was found £5O
worth of Victorian stamps. He has incurred penalties
amounting to over £lOOO.
* * *
In England the pavilion is, during intervals, the
sanctuary of the gentleman against that social outcast,
the professional. In county matches the gentleman
receives £25 as expenses-the pro. gets £5 as wages. The
Brahmin cannot sup or sit down with the Sudra ; yet the
conduct of gentleman W. G. Grace—that champion
exponent of British cricket and British bad manners-so
disgusted Lehmann when the team was bidding Sydney
adieu, that he told Grace straight: “Not for £lOOO per
week would I again join a team captained by you.”
* * #
A week or two ago, in a boxing match at Sam
Matthews’ hall, Sydney, West, one of the combatants,
felled the other—Simpson—with such force, that the arm
on which the battered wretch dropped, snapped near the
elbow. After all, the sole argument in support of the
prize ring is that it calls directly on a man’s brute
courage. No cur can face a foe and fair play. The same
might be said of all the severe man-and-man struggles.
Seldom does a beaten pugilist present the sorry spectacle
that Beach did after his last victory over Hanlan on the
Nepean. The exhausted conqueror, after being lifted out
of his boat, vomited, and trembled like a stricken infant,
mid for nearly half-an-hour could speak only in gasps. He
n rrowly escaped dying in his boat.
# # *
The proudest man seen in Wilcannia for years
was he who bought the finest specimen of a golden eagle
yet brought to town, and tethered him by a chain to a
stump in the back yard. His next-door neighbour was
disturbed at breakfast next morning by the bursting into
the room of his son who breathlessly told him to get his
gun at once as there was an immense eagle on the fence
after the fowls. A few minutes afterwards the most dis-
gusted man in Wilcannia [topped his head over the fence
and in sarcastic tones asked the shooter if lie didn’t feel
proud of his sportsmanship in blowing the head off a bird
chained up with a steel cable?
* * *
Grace, it would appear, expects the “colonials ”
to treat him as Hodge the umpire did in a match against
som“ English bumpkins. Chawbacon, the bowler, sent
the champion’s bails into the air, and the whole field of
clod-hoppers grunted and squealed. “Notout!” blurted
Hodge readjusting the wicket—and then (aside): “You
were clean bowled, sir, and if it happens again I’ll have to
siy Out.”

The draw between Creedon and Costello was a
result of the contest not starting till past ten o'clock —a
mistake often made by club authorities, vho waste time
in preliminary goes.
A ‘tipster” on one of the Sydney weeklies
makes mention of pretty well every likely horse during
the preparation for the principal races, and when each
race is over he comes out with an article on the “ didn’t-
I-tell-you-so ” principle, and harks back to some particular
date on which he drew attention to the winner.

The return match England v. Victoria com-
mences on 17th instead of 18th March, as at first fixed.
The third test-match at Adelaide begins 24th March,
five days being thus available for the last match of the
already-decided rubber. The Englishmen sail for London
on 30th.

imagine.

# # #
If asked to name the greatest game of chance
under the sun, the writer would name cricket. Sid.
Gregory, who played his perfect innings of 93 against the
Englishmen, made 2 on Saturday in a club match.
# # #
Had Zalinski carried a little less flesh for the
Champion Stakes he might never have been caught by
his crack stable companion. The half-brother to Trenton
was not more than a length in front of Highborn and
Strathmore at the last turn, but still he kept pegging
away like a good ’un, and, although palpably eased at the
finish, he passed the post a good three lengths in front of
the one-eyed gelding. Zalinski’s tremendous quarters
and big heart would stand him in good stead uphill or
through mud, but on the hard, dead level he seems to be
slightly outridden by Strathmore, a longer and more
gracefully-built colt.
* * #
As the field tore up the straight for the Free
Handicap the ill-fated Pyg. was within an ace of falling
down and causing another catastrophe. Fortunately
Morrison made a smart recovery and had his horse going
again before there was time for anyone without field-
glasses to discover why Pyg. had so suddenly lost his
place and chance of winning.
# * *
Brodribb, the ex-plunger, is back again, and
was “ doing ” Plemington last week in company with his
buxom wife. He enjoyed the negative satisfaction of
seeing that Titan, although more of a dandy than of yore,
is as complete a failure as when he (Brodribb) sold the
gelding for a couple of thousand less than cost price.
* # #
Gough had no sooner weighed in for the Aus-
tralian Cup than the V.R.C. stewards asked him to explain
Highborn’s running on the Saturday previous. After the
champion race Cusdin was taxed for an explanation of
Strathmore’s defeat in the aforesaid Cup and in each
instance the jockey pleaded “jostling” as an excuse.
Punters, take heed. It “form” is to he constantly
“ interfered ” with in long distance races, when runners
are few, it is about time that the public left off gambling
on the disorderly sprints, for which no horse is a good
thing under present reckless riding conditions, unless
there is a general conspiracy to let the favoured animal
get off in front and stop there.

London Hawk says Slavin is aground for the
want of a big friend. “If anything ever looked well on
paper, it is Slavin, who would easily beat that drunken
bullock, Sullivan, who will not and cannot ever go into
the ring fit to fight.”
* * #
Sporting writers are pertinent in decrying the
ridiculously slow time of the Victorian Champion—one
minute and a half slower than Le Grand’s year. It is
urged that the prize ought not to be given unless the
time be reasonable, but, as it is, they canter two miles.
and only gallop one. The weight-for-age race cannot
hold its ground at all against the handicap, and even
the Epsom Derby will yield the palm.
# * *
A N.Q. board of arbitration was recently dis-
cussing whether £4 or £3 10s. per week should be the
ruling wages at a certain mine. The secretary to the
mine, a giant of 6ft. 3in., suddenly blurted out—“To
(heaven) with all this (vermilion) yabber. I’ll fight all
hands, one down the t’other come on. All those who
lick me get £4 a week; those that I gruel only get £3
10s.” His after comment was “And the blanky
idiots wouldn’t take up the (adjective) gamble.”
* * *
In London a young journalist, wishing to com-
bine sport and religion, has registered a paper to be
called the Christian Sportsman. He should get a wrinkle
or two from Tom Butler, of Sydney Freeman’s Journal,
who has admirably succeeded in wedding true religion to
the odds.

years old will probably soon be too heavy,

One of the selection committee, when rallied for
not picking Sid. Gregory to play against the Englishmen,
said that “ Gregory would make a player in time.” The
time was not long coming, however, as Gregory, when
picked for the next match, played his great innings of 93,
not out.

The latest is that Charley Mitchell has been
matched to fight Fitzsimmons for the middle-weight
championship. We all thought Mitchell had thickened
out beyond that class. Mitchell was a fighter of the first
order. If his fast life has not demoralised him, this
should prove the fight of Fitz’s life.

Hayes and Jones, two prominent Sydney ex-
ponents of the old and much-neglected game of handball,
go to the States by the Alameda. It was at handball that
old-time Jem Belcher had his eye knocked out, and it was
as a worn-out man and a “ boko,” that he tackled his old
pupil Tom Cribb, and was drubbed.

Fitzsimmons, the boxer who beat Irish champion
Maher, is a lanky, scarlet-headed, spindle-shanked middle-
weight. “ Fitzy ” was of a very domesticated turn of
mind before he left for the States, and any Sunday morn-
ing might have been seen stalking down 'the Glebe-road,
Sydney, with Madame by his side, and the yearling Fitzy
in his arms. But oh, what a difference in the States,
where Robert affects the Astrakan overcoat and silk
stovepipe !
“An honest man in politics gets all-fired
lonesome sometimes.” —Amer.
Acting - Coroner Neild, Melbourne, a
semi-demi agnostic, is characteristic and con-
scientious in his caution. A little fellow came up
the other day as a witness, and, says Neild,
sternly, “My boy, if you don’t speak the truth,
you’ll—ahem ! —get into trouble.”
There was a precious row, in Melbourne
and Adelaide, about that Chloe picture, but seven
Chloes, sans clothes, are included in Schrnalz’s
painting of the Virgin Martyrs, at Josh. Lake’s
new Melbourne Exhibition. Yet the ladies flock
around unshocked, because the subject is Holy,
the poor girls being exposed at the Coliseum so
that the lions and tigers could make a handy
meal. This was Edwin Long’s trick—you can
paint what you like if you only take care to give
it a halo. A far better picture than Schmalz’s,
though, is that other nudification, the Pompeiian
sculptor choosing his model, by Pavlin, a young
artist deceased.
.
To Readers of “The Bulletin.”
“Every man can write at least one book,” every
man with brains lias at least one good story to
tell ; every man with or without brains moves in
a different circle and knows things unknown to
any other man. Write carefully and plainly on
one side of the paper only, obliterating every un-
necessary word ; then mail your work to The
Bulletin, which pays for accepted matter. In
certain of the so-called prize-competitions the
great prizes fall to the newspaper-proprietor, who
gets the guileless public to work for him for noth-
ing. Short stories, or ballads, especially on bush,
mining, sporting, social, or dramatic themes, are
preferred by The Bulletin ; 1360 words go to a
column. If you can possibly keep your story
within a column all the better. Don’t write a
column on any subject if a half-column will do ;
don’t write half-a-column where a mere paragraph
is enough. “ Boil it down.”
Suggestions for illustration with or without
rough explanatory drawings will be gladly re-
ceived by The Bulletin.
Chief-steward W. Boulton, of the s.s.
Arawatta, has deserted “the briny” and taken
up a responsible shore position as manager of the
Grosvenor Hotel, Sydney, an office which his
many years of stewardship eminently fits him to
fulfil. Boulton was the “ Commodore ” among
A.U.S.N Co. stewards, and can look back upon
six years of appreciated service with that com-
pany, and eight years’ antecedent service with the
Union S.S. Co.*' Travellers along the Queensland
coast will remember Boulton as chief steward of
the Barcoo and the Arawatta. The A.U.S.N. Co.
has expressed its regret at losing his services, with
a spontaneous avowal that his place will be found
very difficult to fill. The cooks and stewards’
made the occasion of his retirement an opportunity
for presenting him with a valuable souvenir,
memorialising the esteem and high regard in
which they hold him. The ceremony of present-
ing this souvenir—a chastely designed and costly
silver salver— was performed on Ist March by
Second-steward Denny, in the ladies’ saloon of the
Arawatta and in the presenceof about 30stewards
and cooks, and one stewardess, who, by the way,
made a natty little speech in harmony with the
general proceedings. Uelicitations were happily
exchanged and Boulton was so moved emotionally
that everybody had, perforce, to “ wet the other
eye. ”

The Wild-Cat Column.
Pambula has been persistently boomed ; but, to
quote Abigail, what are the facts? Three claims are
getting gold, and more than thirty-three nottiing. Pam-
bula gold is worth £4 an ounce, but it is costing a lot
more to get it. The shoots have no length, and the
striking of a harder belt of country would knock them
out of depth. Too much is made of the returns of ore
sent away for treatment, and too little of the means by
which it is picked. One company, in classifying, use a
horse-trough, in which the ore is scrubbed for specimens
by boys ; consequently, the very best is sent away. The
wrong consists in concealing from the public the methods
by which the big yields per ton are made up. Another
company is getting ore on the “gouging-the-eyes-
out” principle, and each has an abundance of easily-
worked ore from the same lode, that they are apparently
afraid to touch. The parent company have all tiieir best
gold above the 100 ft. level, and, to all appearances, pre-
cious little below'. Nearly all the others are working
themselves out of existence. The local people, with few
exceptions, have had a sad gruelling. There are many in
Sydney, and more in Melbourne, who remember the field
with regret.
# # *
The first men of a new mining-field in N.S.W.
are generally limber-cutters, starved-out selectors and
other bushmen, who take to mining under the blissful
idea that a fortune within a year is an absolute certainty.
They are the holders of the “blocks pegged out for
miles a phrase always seen in the newspapers after
every find of silver or gold. Within a few days two or
three metropolitan boomists make their way to the field.
Sometimes their capital is absorbed in their fine clothes,
and any other deficiency they make up in cheek. They
know a mine when they see it, but the value of the dis-
covery is not of so much importance as the newness of
the field. They are armed with agreements of every form
drawn with the schemer’s eye to business—his own busi-
ness. The blanks only require to be filled in and the
signature attached. They discourse on the depression in
Sydney and underrate everything but themselves. A
magnificent picture of the advantages of paid-up shares,
the cornering of the markets, and the booming of scrip
generally is drawn. Finally the owners, whose belief in
the field is seldom big enough to induce them to have
anything to do with contributing shares, place their
blocks under offer for, say, 12 months, accept £1 deposit,
agree to take no cash but hold the mine in paid-up shares.
The boomsters return to Sydney and issue the balance to
themselves paid up to, say, 19s. 9d., and escape the re-
maining threepence by no-liability registration.
* * *
There are as many ways of salting a mine as of
killing a dog. So far the Last Call plan remains a
mystery though there are those who profess to have a
shrewd idea of the manner in which the rule was run
over Hammond. He was allowed to do what he liked, to
prospect when, and where, he liked. Unfortunately,
however, he did not take aff the precautions which he will
exercise if ever called upon again. It is so easy to be
wise after such an event. Maoriland has been a wonderful
breeding-ground of sailers. The neatest was that in
which a beach sand-drift was represented to contain big
gold. Every reasonable precaution was taken and the
returns were enormous. When the purchase was made
and tenS of thousands changed hands in shares the
mystery was revealed. The examiners took everything
with them but the ore-bags, of which there were plenty on
the mine. Subsequently when the returns fell to zero
microscopical and chemical examinations of the gold from
the best lots demonstrated that it was an alloy of gold—
the silver being in artificial combination with it. The
salter, whoever he was, for like his confreres he had
“ cleared,” had ground a great number of sovereigns and
lined the bags indiscriminately with gold-dust—'-id his
pockets With the results of his inventiveness.
* # *
It was a melancholy gathering. The object was
to hear what the directors had to say concerning the
Last Call mine. We have been at much livelier funerals.
Newton, the chairman, and Hammond, whom many
people had made their mining god, are not the most
cheerful of men under any circumstances. In place of
beards, nature has planted on their perfectly bald faces
immovable seriousness, and they now looked glum. The
features of Bragg belied his name. The usually festive
Daley failed to look even pleasant. Edwards, who
plunged into the “tender shares” to the tune of £5OOO,
wore a vacant, far-away stare, as if just recovering from
a wild dream of dismal unreality. The absent local
director, Urquhart, expressed his feelings in a telegram
of the most mournful description about the prospects of
the mine. Around the room were scattered some thirty
persons whose demeanour became the occasion, and there
was a lamentable absence of persons who were all there
at the early stages of the boom. The chairman told a
tale of sorrow and regret. Reduced to a few words, it
was that they put their money in, believing that their
fortunes were made. He reminded them of their pre-
vious meeting, which was understood to be of quite a
different character, when even Hammond was gleeful at
the prospect of coming riches. He put it plainly that
the mine had been deliberately salted, and that they had
been the victims of well-planned sooundrelism ; their
only hope was that the gold used for this purpose would
be found elsewhere in the mine. Not a word was
said about the criminals and no hint given as to what
steps were to be taken to convict them. A man who
described himself as a heavy loser complimented the
directors for doing their utmost in the interests of the
shareholders under the circumstances, and another who it
is well know’ll got his shares for services rendered, asked a
great many asinine questions. His faith in the property
passeth all understanding. Because he had taken some
rich specimens from the top of the drive he refused to
believe that the bottom had fallen out of the mine. He
conveyed the idea that if the bowels of the earth were
pierced in a great many places to immeasurable depths,
something might be found. In the might only the meet-
ing agreed. IBs questions ranged from ttie value of gold
to the cost of Mr. Bragg’s trip, while Hammond listened
in a way that indicated that life would have been worth
living if lie hadn’t met the Last Call crowd. The
meeting began, continued, and ended in misery. What
is wanted now’ is a meeting of all those who sold the
shaves at £2 and got clear out. We believe that would
be a cheerful meeting and would break up in sheer
hilarity.
* * *
Do not hesitate to show any man the door who
wants you to go into a machine which he claims will con-
centrate well without classification. Perfect concentra-
tion without minute classification is as impossible as sight
without light.
*‘ # *
Luhrig’s the new German concentrator,
which is also a classifier, is, as far as can be gleaned from
eulogistic notices, very imported. Tested in the presence
0 f the inventor and, of course, under other favourable
conditions, a loss of 27 per cent, is admit ed.
* * *
Having concentrated a parcel of White Rock
ore, it is declared that the mine, by the use of this con-
centrator, can be made topay handsomely if the parcel sent
represents the average of the mine. If. The Bulletin,
observing the qualification after hearing many assurances
that it is the average, stands up tq say that the
value of the White Rock ore in hulk appears to be under
lOozs, of silver to the ton.
# * #
Be not ass enough to believe the old familiar
wheeze that the man makes the mine—it is the mine
which makes the man. The ablest manager will lose his
reputation in a had mine, while the veriest duffer will
make his in a good one.

their trousers.

The thirst for gold and other precious metals
has subdued. What, is wanted now by many who thirsted
ln the boom is something to eat.
# # #
The burglar who recently took away a quantity
of scrip under the impression that they were bank-notes,
r ,L serv * n S of 11111(3,1 sympathy. Had be taken the
liability also he would have been ruined.
A warning—ln Bendigo after 1871 some scrip
which cost the owner £lO,OOO was sold for a five-pound
note. And the purchaser thereby became insolvent.
* # •#
“The result is highly satisfactory inasmuch as
the crushing included a lot of mullock,” is an oft-
recurring newspaper mining memo. It is always rubbish
or mullock when the result is “ sick.”
* * *
“Firsts,” “seconds,” and “thirds,” as crushings
are often described in N,S.W., are unknown in Charters
Towers, Gympie, Bendigo, Ballarat, or any other legiti-
mate gold-mining centre. Ninety-nine times out of a
hundred gold is thus classified for stock-jobbing purposes.
* * *
The poverty-stricken character of half the lodes
in the countiy is concealed in other excuses, such as
“the ore is too refractory for ordinary treatment;”
“ most of the gold is contained in the pyrites;” “the
battery lost the gold because of its extreme fineness.”
The ghastly truth that the results are poor because of the
absence of gold is never told.
# * *
The great desideratum in our mining-fields is to
get ores good enough to treat, not machinery good
enough to treat them. However, a machine to extract
gold from goldless ores is a long-felt want.
Someone notes that the Times suffers from
“chronic inability to make an apology.” On the
principle, we suppose, that the Deity never
retracts.

London Weekly Bulletin on the litera-
ture of the English people
Anybody taking the trouble to wonder why the nation
is in the last throes of dry-rot need not go further than to
the little newspaper seller of his nearest village. He will
find on the counter when the newspaper train arrives per-
haps a bare half dozen of such journals as Reynolds’s
News or the Weekly Dispatch (thoroughly progressive
papers). But he will find that every living soul in the
neighbourhood subscribes towards a collection of “things”
(we cannot coin a word bad enough for them) which
would disgrace the intellect of a bargee’s idiot offspring.
The Christian World, the Church Bells, the oh,
our rage stops our going further with the catalogue, but
everyone of them is a cringe, cringe, cringe to someone
in power. “Letters from Jerusalem,” By the Rev. Mr.
Snooks; “A lecture on Nazareth,” by the Rev. Mr.
Brown; “ A Chapter on our Redeemer,” by Canon
Tomkins, etc., etc., etc. Any rot we can imagine—all
with an aim and object, viz., that of cadging. Cadging
for a bishop, cadging for a new church with its necessary
endowment, cadging for well, something ,or other.
And every cadge means a “commission.” Can we
wonder at our insular ignoranc' when the British
intellect is fed upon such pabulum as we have described 1!
Why in the Western States of America every village
would have made a bonfire of such rubbish. There they
take in the “Prairie Farmer,” the “Mechanics’ Maga-
zine,” the “ Artisans’ Fade Mecum And then they send
us apples, poultry, meat, wheat, corn, oats, hay—any-
thing we like to mention. And don’t they laugh in their
sleeves w’hen they look at the list of our trashy, wishy-
washy literature that teaches of a ridiculous past and
impossible future.
Young Montague F. Levey, son of rich
old Montague Levey, of Sydney, deceased, turned
up on Ist. March, at Melbourne insolvency court,
and made a great effort to explain where his little
pile had gone to. It was a very successful effort
in its way. Young Levey’s fortune consisted of
=£4oo per annum and a deferred inheritance of
£17,000 due about 18 months hence. He also
received £3,200 in cash, out of which his trustee—
who was also his brother-in-law—stopped £l,lOO
for some old debts. Then the garish youth started
to keep racehorses, and gently “blewed” the other
=£2loo. The man who sold him the racehorses
was his particular factotum, and advised him on
things generally. They gambled together, and
the factotum won. When Levey owed him about
.£l5OO, he also helped him to sell out his interest
under his father’s will; the factotum was prac-
tically the seller, and the insolvent's sister was
the buyer, and the brother-in-law —a Potts
Point magnate, by the way—acted as the
sister’s agent. Among them, the =£4oo
per annum and the =£17,000, which was
nearly due, were brought up for =£3300 in cash.
“ Financial operations in Sydney,” remarked the
brother-in-law blandly, “ are not conducted on a
five per cent, basis,” which seems a self-evident
assertion. Then Levey’s factotum collected his
outstanding little bill, and the young man went
forth into a stone-cold world, and spent the rest
of his money, and then broke. The moral —
addressed to old Levey in his sepulchre—is that
no rich elderly Hebrew should allow his son to
come of age until he is 40.

WOMEN AND FIREARMS.
The condition of terror to which the sight of a
mouse, a cockroach, or a pistol reduces a woman
is phenomenal; yet the same frail creature can
stand the ordeal of a marriage ceremony or a
christening without the slightest embarrassment,
while the natural protector of “ the sex,” who
under ordinary circumstances looks big and talks
bigger, cannot face either of the two last mentioned
trials without feeling fifty ways at once, and
experiencing a sensation that causes him to
devoutly wish that a good healthy earthquake
would come along and swallow up all that is left
of his diminished former self. But show the
average woman a gun or a pistol, and she will
shrink away and cry “Oh, dear,” and “Oh,
don’t,” and “ Oh, for mercy sake,” and will tremble
like a leaf even though the weapon be without
lock, stock, or barrel. With a man however —
how different! He will take up the shooting-iron
with as much nonchalance as he would sample a
first-class brand of whiskey, cock it, peer into the
muzzle with a critical expression in his eye, try
the spring of the trigger, and—finish a good
jug job for the doctor or the undertaker.
There can be no doubt about it; man in number-
less ways shows Ids superiority over woman.
Seriously speaking, though, the opportunity of
women, and with it her bravery and fortitude,
comes in the hour when trouble or sickness affects
those who are dear to her. Then it is that she
becomes a ministering angel, in whose keeping the
well-being and safety of a household depends, and
no prudent housewife ever neglects to keep in
stock a bottle or two of St. Jacobs Oil ready for
use in the event of accident or disease overtaking
either herself, the breadwinner, or one of the olive
branches of the family. A great trait in woman’s
character is that she is ever ready to praise the
bridge that carries her over—hence the following
unsolicited testimonial from Mrs. Calvert, of
Darlington: “I have suffered a great deal from
rheumatics in my hands and shoulders. I have
tried a great many things for it, but have found
nothing but St. Jacobs Oil to do me any good.”
Tins renowned medicine may be had of any
druggist or storekeeper.

A Golden Shanty.
Hans-A Condensed Story.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
In the early clays of a small mining town-
ship hidden away in a remote corner of the
sequestered wilds of dear old Maoriland there
was an abandoned shaft some 90 feet deep. One
day an enterprising individual covered it over
with saplings. Shortly afterwards a mass of tail-
ings obliterated the whole, and at this stage our
story opens.
There had arrived in the town an old German
(almost noseless), named Hans. He was a man of
gigantic build, but already past the prime of life.
Whatever accident had befallen his proboscis, it
seemed to have had its effect upon his whole
being. There is no physical injury so socially
disastrous as visible damage to the nose. If your
nose happens to have been knocked to one side,
people regard you as an ex-prize fighter. It is
useless to explain that you collided with a cricket-
ball when you were young. If you are bereft of
portion of your nose, people look at you and think
of an ourang-outang or a hangman. Hans’ face
boro traces of having, at one time, been generous
and open, but it was as if his better nature had
shrunk back upon itself until he had become as
gloomy and as sullen and as sodden and as morose
and as sour and as dispirited as it was possible to
be. His taciturn disposition found a suitable
camping-ground somewhat away from the rest
upon the summit of a knoll of tailings.
There he lived some three or four years. None
knew his history. Ho was a strange mixture of
atheist, pessimist, and socialist. He had but one
intimate friend —a youth of French extraction
with strong republican sentiments and of a gay
and restlessly festive spirit. The two were drawn
towards each other by similar political views and
were now bosom friends.
* * #
On the evening when this story begins—
and ends —they were conversing together, Hans
had dropped his taciturnity for once, and was
telling the story of his life. It was just about
sunset; the grey shadows were stealing over
the waste places of the old goldfield ; and
amid the gathering dusk the noseless German
gesticulated wildly on his pile of tailings. It was
the old tale—one girl and two suitors. First, she
had declared in favour of Hans, who was an I
atheist. However, the other fellow prayed for
divine assistance, and the result was most gratify-
ing, for the Almighty had planted a cancer in the
nose of Hans. The doctor cut it out, but thereby
spoiled the facial contour of his patient. His
rival drew inferences from the circumstances :
partly by reason of his deformity, partly through
religious scruples—the girl left Hans ; and he had
wandered to Australia.
# # #
The evening deepened. The damper was
almost done. The holy Sabbath calm was an
emblem of peace. The youth, who was lying on
the bunk, had just read from the newspaper an
article, describing how a steamer had been gutted
as she lay in the dock. “ Ha,’’said Hans, gloomily,
“dere vas dat phine shdeemer; de ghautz vas
bhorned glean oud mid er. De Almidy vas at it
vouch more.”
* * *
These were his last words. The Almighty
was at it once more. The earth trembled—a
caving noise was heard. In a moment, Hans, the
frying-pan, the chimney, the damper and the fire
had disappeared. The poor fellow essayed to
clutch the bunk, but missed, and rattled down
with the rest. The chimney had been built over
the abandoned shaft.
* * *
Two days after, Hans was buried. The
cinders had even scorched his deformed nose in
death. He was only one of millions whom Fate
remorselessly pursues through life. Yet, when-
ever I hear of an appalling catastrophe, I cannot
help thinking of Hans’ last words —and, like
Victor Hugo, I would wish to know why so many
destinies are driven pell-mell upon blazes.
POLYPE.
Years ago, when the Otago Central (M.L.)
railway was first mooted, a commission was sent
out to explore the wastes of the interior .and
report on the prospects. Vincent Pyke accom-
panied the expedition in the double capacity of
'ocal member and guide, and Pyke was resolved
that the report should be favourable at any cost.
For four days the commissioners struggled along
in buggies over rocky, boulder-strewn highways,
through long, uninviting wastes of tussocks and
dust, and at night it put up at wretched bush
pubs., kept by unkempt publicans with shock
aeads and door-mats under their chins. But at
every pub. the cranky deal table groaned under
a burden of fruit and flowers, and the mutton
was splendid, and the vegetables made the com-
mission’s mouth water. “ All these things are
grown in the neighbourhood,” Pyke would
observe blandly. “ They grow everywhere here,
though, of course, you can’t expect to see them as
you drive along the road.” The commissioners
fed luxuriously, and drafted a report that Central
Otago, though it looked somewhat desolate and
sterile as viewed from the road, was a land of
wondrous fertility and ought to have a railway at
once. But the fifth day a disaster happened. In
addition to the other fruit and the flowers and
vegetables there were sundry splendid pineapples
on the table. “Are these grown here?” asked
the fat commissioner smacking his lips ; “Acres of
them,” answered Pyke cheerfully ; “ though,
of course, they aren’t visible from the road.”
“And are the ahem tickets grown here
also,” demanded the fat commissioner. Pyke
looked at the ticket; it read: “Vincent Pyke,
Esq., fromß, Smith, fruiterer, Dunedin.” “Yes,”
he responded, with an absolutely unmoved coun-
tenance ; “in good seasons the crop of tickets in
this district is something amazing.” The commis-
sioners looked at him hard, and he looked hard at
the commissioners, and neither of them said any-
thing. But a report was promptly drawn up
which blasted the Central Otago railway, and it
remains in a blasted state to this day; and Pyke
as promptly stopped the supplies, so that all the
way back the commission lived chiefly on salt goat
and bad potatoes. Wonder whether Pyke will
have the impudence to deny the truth of this story.
