THE BEALE PATENT
HIGH ARM SEWING MACHINES
Have, by their continued merits and the general
satisfaction they have invariably given, won the
first place in public esteem, and are on all sides
admitted to be the best and most useful sewing
machines in the market.
FOR USE IN THE HOUSEHOLD
The Beale Machine presents innumerable advant-
ages. The mechanism and the adjustments are
perfect, while the working is most simple; and
the addition of all the most recent inventions and
improvements renders the manipulation of every-
thing connected with the machine a matter of
perfect ease. The result of this is, that with
ordinary care the machine will last for many
years, nothing can get out of order, and no time
is wasted in tiresome meddling with the parts.
It is a servant that will never grumble, never
rebel, never want nights out, willing even on
washing days. It only asks for a careful cleaning
now and then, a snug, dry corner and a little
genuine sewing machine oil. Any other oil will
make it sick, and dirt of all kind it abhors.
The Self-threading 1 Shuttle, of solid steel,
saves the trouble of threading by the end of the
cotton. It has an excellent tension, is strong and
simple.
The Bobbin Winder is a marvel of in-
genuity, winding and guiding the cotton by
itself, as evenly as upon the reel whence it came,
and stopping of its own accord when the bobbin
is full.
For all kinds of family sewing and fancy the
Beale Machine has the greatest capacity.
The Improved High Arm. A great de-
sideratum with ladies has been the raising of the
arm of the machine, so as to allow the goods to be
sewn freely to pass underneath. This had to be
accomplished without increasing the weight of the
machine. In fact, the Beale runs with wonderful
ease and rapidity. It is almost silent, all clicking
sounds having been obviated. As is well known,
a good test of the finish of any mechanism is its
silence when in rapid motion.
With the Wonderful Beale Machine,
whether for hand or foot use, you can hem any
width, make double seams or fells, braid stitch
with chenille, Berlin wool, or gold cord, and such
like fancy work; you may make a pretty lace
stitch, tuck ever so finely, bind dresses, coats, or
carpets ; sew on trimmings without guiding, make
frilling, ruffling, or niching; quilt, weave your
own braid with any combination of colours and
stitch it on at the same time ; make thick, woollen
door-mats, embroidered antimacassars, window
curtains, and so forth ; you can easily and rapidly
perform the fashionable shirring or gathering so
popular for sleeves, bodies, or skirts of dresses
with an appliance supplied gratis ; lay the small
cords in hoods and stays ;do large cording; gather
with or without a band, and generally do all
kinds of straight sewing upon any material what-
ever. You can darn upon any woven goods, and
do crewel-work. The Beale Patent will pass
from eight folds of tweed or twenty of calico to
two of thinnest muslin, or even mosquito-net or
tulle, without the operator changing needle,
cotton, stitch, or tension, and the fine material
will not be gathered in the slightest. You may
make button .and eyelet-holes, do over-casting,
make herring-bone stitch upon any materials.
This work is absolutely unique and can only be
accomplished upon the Beale Patent Machine.
Any of these performances will be shown at our
shops at any time. The Beale Patent makes
a lock-stitch, which cannot ravel, yet by a momen-
tary alteration the sewing can be done so as to
rip from end to end, if desired. This peculiarity
is sometimes useful when children’s clothes are
intended to be altered.
Nickel-plating. —In our climate the exposed
parts of the machine, and the applicances, need
to be protected from rust. We have, at consider-
able expense, added a handsomely nickeled wheel,
nickeled plates, screws, appliances, treadle-bar
and cones. Heightening the appearance, these
improvements also tend largely to preserve the
parts. This plating is imperishable.
A special feature of The Beale Machine is
that
ALL THE CABINET WORK
is made at our factory in Sydney from registered
designs, and of the choicest
AUSTRALIAN WOODS,
some of which are without doubt the most beauti-
ful in the world. Suitability to the climate is
thus insured, and we are enabled to offer a greater
variety of styles, most handsome in design and
conveniently fitted for all the requirements of a
lady’s work-table.
These machines can be inspected at any time at
our principal show-rooms in Sydney, or at any of
our suburban and country branch shops, or from
travellers who are continually visiting all parts.
All the machines may be had upon easy time-
payment, or for cash less three shillings in the
pound.
Photographs, Price-Lists and Samples
FREE BY POST.
No matter where you live, we will supply
you with a Sewing Machine upon easy Time-
Payment.

The Western People’s after-howl against
tho rival editor : —
He would go a little further if he dare—he would raise
and flaunt the poisoned coils which may be seen in his
glassy leer and his hypocritical smirk, but he knows to
do' that would mean his own fall, not ours.
An aboriginal rouseabout on a back-blocks
station had a kangaroo-dog of which he was very
proud, until one day in a hunt it was beaten out
of time by the stumpy-tailed cur of one of the
boundary riders. Jacky pondered deeply on this,
an d made up his mind that the superiority of the
white man’s dog was due to the abbreviation of
its tail. He speedily chopped off his dog's narra-
tive, and as horrified canine fled for
the skyline and vanished, Jacky ejaculated, “ Too
flurry short, too flurry short me cut him.” Jacky
had made his dog too speedy.
A tale is going the rounds anent Ted
Nathan and the old coaching days. A man applied
to Nathan for a billet as a coachman on the
N.S.W. Western road. “ Got any references ? ”
“No, but I have never yet upset a coach since I first
handled a rein.” “Then you’re no (bally) good
for this (ruddy) road, for when a (carroty) coach
did (purple well) upset, you wouldn’t know how
to get the (thumping) thing right-side-up again.
Go round to Jones ; he wants a (sanguinary) soft-
tomrny swell of pious (polyglot) principles and
(doggoned) education to (roaring well) ringbark
Bathurst burrs. And you’d suit him right down
to the(gory) ground.”
At Geraldton (Q.) not long since a miser-
able, broken-down Javanese labourer crawled
wretchedly into the town from an outlying plan-
tation in search of medical assistance. He had
been taken ill with dysentery, and had been
accommodated in the hovel which was dignified
on the plantation by the name of an “hospital.”
Tor some time he was an “in-patient” at this
place —which means presumably that he was
allowed to lie down in it; and after that he
became an “ out-patient,” which is probably
another way of saying that ho was allowed to put
his head in and get his medicine. Unhappily, he
grew worse instead of better, and in a panic
he struggled off to Geraldton, where the
Government Health Officer promptly sent him
to hospital. Close behind him came an official
who took out a warrant for the arrest of this
emaciated spectre on a charge of going to the
hospital, or in other words, “deserting his hired
service.” Finding, however, that his half-dead
nigger was too sick to be captured, he withdrew
the warrant and, instead, served him with a
summons, which strange document was duly
deposited in the miserable object’s bunk. The
case came on, but defendant was too ex-
hausted to attend, so the matter was post-
poned. A few days later, when he was able to
drag himself out of bed, he was helped down to
the court-house, where, being unable to under-
stand the language, and too weak to say anything
if he had understood it, and having no one to
speak on his behalf, he was duly fined £2, with
the alternative of seven days gaol. No wonder
white labour is scarce on the Northern sugar-hells.
The only white labourers the planters will engage
are those who will submit to bo arrested every
time they let go the handle of the hoe, and that
kind of Australian is in short supply at present.

of constipation, dyspepsia, liver complaints, head-

eluding fevers and inflammation of the lungs, and
of other internal organs—not by attacking these
so-called diseases directly, but by radically neutra-
lising and removing their causes, thus allowing
nature herself to do her work unimveded.
In selling this Pamphlet we are obliged to re-
require a “Pledge of Honor” from those purchas-

rights in the discovery can be maintained.
Doctors, of course, who purchase are not debarred
from using the treatment in their own practice,
providing they do not show the pamphlet or do not
reveal the rationale of the treatment.

The Brown Tent.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“ I don’t know who she is, nor how she came,
true, but there she is. All I can tell you is that
one evening when we came back rather late from
our claim, we found her tent had been fixed up
since the morning, and the little woman was out-
side, cooking some savoury-smelling mess over the
lire. ”
Thus Dick Thomas, in response, to my enquiry
as to his new neighbour, while I halted by the
log-tire to light my pipe. It was a remarkable
tent, being of a dull brown colour, tanned pro-
bably with wattle-bark. There seemed to be no
other inmates—in fact, there were none—only the
woman, a delicate little creature who seemed all
too small to be alone on the diggings, fighting the
battle of life unaided. A plucky little woman she
was though, for she got folks’ washing and mend-
ing to do, and when not otherwise employed she
would tuck up her skirts and go about with a tin
dish, looking for gold. Her speech had a slightly
foreign accent, popularly supposed to be redolent
of North Britain, but Dick assured me it was
French. And Dick knew, for, in his masterful
way, ho took this waif, whom he called Ada,
under his protection, getting work for her, fetch-
ing her wood, putting her in the way to find
“patches” of gold, and making it generally under-
stood, that it anyone offered her insult, or caused
her annoyance, they would have to reckon with
Mr. Dick Thomas' six feet of muscular mas-
culinity.
This went on for some time. Ada —no one ever
heard of her other name—made few acquaintances
and talked but little. Her manner was that of
habitual melancholy. She seldom smiled and her
firmly-compressed lips and square chin told of
determination and the power of endurance.
“Dick,” said I, one day, “that is a woman
with a history.”
“ That’s likely,” assented he, “ but, whatever it
i<, nobody can screw it out of her. I have tried
to pimp her myself many times, but she is always
on her guard and I can get nothing out of her.
She won’t oven say where she comes from, though
from her speech and other little things I feel sure
she is of French origin wherever she was brought
up.”
“Don’t let her make a fool of you,” said I,
“these strange women are kittle cattle to deal
with.”
For I was anxious that Dick Thomas, whose
family I had known in Wales, should not get
entangled by this mysterious stranger, who had
plumped down so suddenly amongst us on the
Avoca. But he only pooh-poohed my friendly
counsel and continued to hover about the brown
tent more than seemed necessary or desirable.
“ Indeed then,” cried his mate, Dan Rice, “ he
is there too much and too often, and I have told
him so. Yes, indeed, but he will not heed what I
say. ” _
“Mind your own business, both of you,”
growled Dick. “ I can take erre of myself.”
But he could not, as the sequel proved. Her
bright eyes—for they wore bright, the brightest I
ever saw, I think, but they gleamed, rather than
shone—her bright eyes, I say, drew poor Dick like
a loadstone. There was no laughter in them,
sadness always, tenderness sometimes, but mirth
never.
Just at that time Simpson’s rush broke out—it
is called Maryborough now—and it was settled
that I and Dan. Rice should go over there and
spy out the land. We were lucky in getting on
claims right on “the lead,” and at once sent over
for our mates, as it would not do to leave the spot
for a day. “ Jumping ” was very much iii vogue
at Simpson’s, ami woe betide those who neglected
their ground. Well, my own mates all came over
in a body, but no Dick Thomas. When they went
to see him about shifting there was no Dick to be
found. The tent was fastened up, and his swag
had disappeared. The brown tent and its inmate
bad disappeared also. The inference was obvious.
They had gone away together.
Years passed away before I mot Dick again.
But I did meet him—at Heffernan’s, in Bendigo.
It was in the concert-room, where Thatcher and
Madame Vi belli were doing a good stroke of
business at that time. He did not see me, and I
bad an opportunity of observing him while Madame
was delighting the audience by her arch rendering
of “The Captain with the Whiskers.” He stood
near the doorway, sombre amidst the laughing
crowd ; and in the interval that followed, whilst
“refreshments” were being brought in, I made
my way to him. “ Why, Dick !’ I exclaimed,
placing my hand on his arm—“ Dick Thomas; is
it really you in the flesh ?”
He started and threw off my hand, rather
roughly. Then, recognising me—“Oh ! it’s you !”
he cried, extending his own hand, and grasping
mine heartily. “ Well, lam glad to meet you.
I am on my way Home, and— But let us get
out of this infernal uproar, and go somewhere
where we can talk quietly.”
We went up to Abbott’s, where I was staying,
and got a private room. Then ho told me his
story.
“I know what you are going to ask,” he said.
“ Yes, I did go off with Adele. We used to call
her Ada, you know, but Adele was ber true name.
She had agreed to elope with me when a chance
should occur, and Dan’s going to Simpson’s with
you was right into our hands. We went away as
far as the Ovens, and finally camped at Mitta
Mitta. And there we lived till—till she left me.”
“Left you? Then she” —-—
“Yes, left me—for ever. Poor girl! She is
dead.”
A question was on my lips, but I did not ask it.
He forestalled me,
“No,” he said, “we were never married. God
knows it was not my fault. I offered—begged
her—over and over again to marry me; but she
would not. She was a Catholic, and I was a Pro-
testant, but it was not that alone that prevented
us from being lawfully united. She had a secret
reason, poor soul! which she would not —did not
disclose until it was too late. All she answered
when I urged her, was that she would be a good
wife to me in all but the name, but she would not
marry any son of man. Queer, you say, but
recollect she had peculiar French notions, and
there was a reason of which I know now and
respect her memory for. When she was on her
deathbed she told me to look, after she was gone,
in a small box, of which she gave me the key, and
that there I should find something which would
explain all. ‘ You know, Dick,’ she said, ‘ I have
never really loved you. My love is in Heaven —
I could not love another. But I do like you,
Dick, very much. I always did like you. How
could I help it when you were so good to me, so
kind, so comforting, when I had not a living
friend ? ’ ”
Dick bent his head on his arms and literally
sobbed aloud. And I had no word of sympathy
to offer him; for I was stricken dumb in the
presence of his great grief. Presently he re-
sumed :
“ She was as much my wife as if a score of
bishops had blessed'our union. And I loved her
—ah, boy! I worshipped her. But I never
heard her laugh in all the years we were together.
When I opened her box I understood all, as she
said I would. There was the story of hep life, and
her abiding misery. She was of French parentage,
but had been brought up in England by an aunt
who was settled there. She became the wife of a
gay young fellow of good family, whose name I
must not mention. The marriage was not
approved by his friends, and they sent the young
couple out to Australia to get rid of them In
Sydney he fell in with a fast set and took to
drinking and gambling. One night over a game
of cards, in his own house, a dispute occurred,
angry words were exchanged, and he killed a man.
At least he was charged with it, and the jury
found him guilty, but Adele with her last breath
declared he was innocent—that—oh ! how can I
tell it? Here —read this—just the last para-
graph.”
He took from his pocketbook a much-stained
and blotted manuscript—blotted probably by
tears—and handed it to me, and I read as fol-
lows :
“ —had got my husband down on the sofa, and
was trying to strangle him, or I thought so. I was mad
with tenor, and I seized the pistol and fired it at him. I
did not mean to kill him, hut I did. My husband, ,
is innocent of all participation in his death. lam the
guiltv one; and this 1 swear by all my hopes of eternal
rest.”
Then followed a prayer for her husband’s pardon
and release, signed ‘ ‘ Adele . ”
It was the copy of a petition which she had pre-
sented to the Governor. She took it herself, and
on her knees besought mer - cy for her husband,
affirming her own guilt and offering to suffer in
his place.. But it was all in vain ; the poor wretch
was hanged, and Adele was placed in a lunatic
asylum, for the doctors declared she was mad, and
at the time perhaps she was.
“ Do you believe she did it?” I asked.
“ My dear fellow,” said Dick, “ the wound was
in front. How could she have shot him there, in
the position the men were ? But she repeated the
statement so often, that at last she came to believe
it. She would have sacrificed herself to save her
husband, but he would not suffer it. When he
heard that his wife had accused herself to screen
him, he was manly enough to confess that his own
hand had launched the fatal bullet. But what
will not a woman do for the man she loves?”
TAATARA.
A Gulf-country horror : One, M'Dougalh
owns a crippled daughter and a decrepit tilt-
waggon. The girl is blind and dumb, paralysed
in her arms, and has only rudimentary feet, with
which she manages to feed herself. M‘Dougall
was taking this deformed misery in the almost
equally-deformed tilt-waggon from Georgetown to
Hughenden, via Oak Park and Chndleigh Park.
The shandrydan broke down about nine miles
from Chudleigh Park. Leaving the girl in the
waggon, the father started to ride the distance.
The station is off the road, and he missed it, and
rode wearily on to Mount Sturgeon, 35 miles
further, the manager of which showed him the
way to Chudleigh. Meanwhile, the crippled girl
was left three days without food. She had rolled
herself out of the waggon in her agony. They
found her 20 yards away, covered with flies and
with myriads of ants devouring her, Ilowever,
she still lives.
Cur Republic.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Gazins; afar, we have glimpses of infinite glory ;
Dimly as yet, but suggesting a brightness untold ;
Full of a sweetness unknown, and unhinted in story—
What shall it be when the mists of the morn are
uproll'd ?
Do ! Our Republic, afar, but each day drawing nearer ;
Proud in the knowledge of strength and the glory of
might.
See ! she is looming up larger, and standing out clearer ;
Fair is her form, and her face is ineffably bright.
Bright is her glance, in the knowledge of future dominion,
Clear is her brow, and firm-set is her beautiful mouth ;
Mighty her limbs, which no vile old-world shackles shall
pinion ;
Naught shall enslave Our Republic, the Pride of the
South.
Hark ! On our anvils the plates of her harness are ringing;
Bright shall it gleam when enclasping each radiant
limb ;
Hear how the armour-smiths gaily are shouting and
singing,
Thundering joyously out the Republican hymn.
“ Freedom ” and “ Freedom ” alone shall she have for a
watchword ;
“ Freedom,” and let it ring out to the ends of the earth;
Not a mere name, not a sound sent about for a catch-
word—
Freedom for all, whatsoever their colour or birth.
Bright shall tier banner be, stainless, a mark to the
nations
(Not like their blood-sodden rags, with , their mem’rics
of war),
Tolling of hope for the countless unborn generations,
Marking a land that is Freedom’s from shore unto
shore.
Oh ! she is coming. Oh ! lovely, and fair as the morning,
Soon shall she reign “ from the centre all round to the
sea,”
Fair is the star of each province her forehead adorning—
Hail, Our Republic, Australia, United and Free.
Melbourne, 23/2/92.

There is practically no limit to the dis-
tance at which conversation can be maintained by
the telephone provided the wire be withdrawn
from proximity to the earth which operates in conn
teraction. The sensitiveness of the electric current
is so great that were a wire stretched from the
earth to the moon the persons at each extremity
would be able to hear one another more distinctly
than by any ordinary short-lined service con-
structed.

John Wilson’s Oath: A Temperance Tale.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Preface and Apology.
I have an aunt, she is pious and tea-total; like-
wise a grand-aunt, who is piouser and teatotaller.
But these ladies have sense (of a sort), and know
that most temperance tracts are too skim-milky
for those whom they would benefit. Tiierefore
at a great mission (I believe that is the correct
word), they proposed that a more realistic author
than usual should be induced, for an honorarium,
to write something that would wind itself into the
sympathies of the ordinary young heathen tippler,
not the general swiper, but the “ wont-go-home.’
till-morning ” lot, the knocker-wrencher, the sign-
board changer, the latch-key man—in fact, the
middle-class d la Moorhouse moderate drinker.
They then named a rising old man called Mr.
Allspice Fmem—in fact, myself. The cunning
old tabbies never said I was their relation, and
they considered that they were giving me a great
chance. In the words of the (nursery) poet
Come, children, listen to my tale.
T -P-—I did not receive the honorarium (fine
word that).
Chap. I.— Analysis : The First Glass.
John Wilson was in love. A man must be in
love when he walks round the slums of Glasgow
with an energetic cousin, visiting men and women
who don’t want to be visited, giving tracts that
are never read, and by a spiteful process are now
made incombustible and therefore unavailable for
pipe-lights. But Helen Mary Henman, the
cousin, was fair to look upon and said she loved
John, which statement is easiest written with a
big L and a little “e ” with one vowel between.
One day the visit was to the “ Gorbals,” a word
which I like because it was used by me in my
youth for young sparrows. There was a fiend of a
male friend with them, a man from somewhere
about the coaly lands. He was a lecturer on what
he called Tottle Obstanins, and just brimful of
conceit. Fate led them to enter a school, and that
was the ruin of poor John Wilson. You could
never guess how school-inspecting, with a pretty girl
and an ugly prig, was the means of the commence-
ment of the crescendo scale of minions of alcohol.
So I'll tell you.
Mr. M'Micking (that was his name) was posi-
tively trying to show off before Nelly Denman •
trying, in fact, to cut out Jack Wilson, and the
mischievous minx was stringing him on. When
the specially well-trained class of boys and girls
had answered the “Chief End of Man,” the
“ Effectual Calling,” and the “ Reasons Annexed,”
the following poser was put without any warning •
“Ma chuldrin, what’s anollisis?” None of them
knew, though a small boy hazarded that it was a
whale. Then with a compassionate smile the
examins.?; told them he’d show them.
“ Hc/e’s a sentence ‘ Joan Wulson bocht
some aypuls to make his chuldrin healthy and
nappy.’”
Oh ! Mi. M'Micking, if you had only known
how neeir you came to being kicked, I fancy you’d
have let the subject, predicate, and object alone.
But the climax. “ Now what were the results :
pheesical, health; moral, happiness?” About this
/ieriod John Wilson was drinking his first gless of
u-husky in a neighbouring public-hoose!
Chap. II. — The Downfall.
Nothing that I can pen is adequate to describe
the fascination of drink. No matter whether
spuuts, wine or ale, the supply creates the demand.
Ibis is a question in political economy ably argued
by politicians, who must have put the theory into
practice. Thus it was with Johnny, long called
cheery, now beery Johnny Wilson. Instead of
the bright, active, manly youth, a leering, untidy,
lurching fellow was seen daily, any time after
lunch, knocking, as it is called, about the town,
till conscience told Nelly that it was her mission
(there you are again) to save this lost sheep. I
must tell you that Miss Denman had a snug little
fortune and more in prospectu, but it was the
woman, not the money, that was the prize. Then
this plucky girl caught the backslider one
day by the arm and took him into a ladies’
restaurant to ask him what he meant? The
answer was ambiguous, not to say irrelevant •
“ whisky and soda.” What did he mean ? Simply
that he was drunk and wanted to be drunker.
Nellie would not allow that, however, and en-
treated him with tears to promise amendment,
saying that anything she could do she would in
order to help him. “Well, then,” said Johnny,
“marry me.” At this the lady hastily left the
place, leaving Mr. Wilson to pay 2s. 3d. for straw-
berries and cream with.
Chap. lII.— A Squatter.
There is a small station in Riverina, near
enough to a railway-station for mail-purposes and
only 10 miles from the town of Curly ville. Until
last year the lessee was known round about as
Quicksilver” Wilson : not that he had discovered
a lode of cinnabar or had invented a new process
for saving gold (that’s rather good) but because lie
was always on the jump. He never waited for
the telegram from Melbourne stating: “Long
between drinks.” His command was—
“ Drinks, boss; quick, like quicksilver.” And
this was the wreck of cheery John Wilson. I
will not say he was in bad health or out of
funds; but morally and mentally ho was not
a bit like the Reverend Gilbert M'Micking.
I cannot state positively that he was often
intoxicated, because drink is so deceitful. Some
can carry it, while many lie down to it. But
everything has an end. A simple sentence in a
school-house caused the first glass of whisky in a
public-house, and a simple sentence in a letter was
the means of a resolution never to drink again in a
tavern.
Chap. IV. —A Love-Letter.
My dear John, —Reports have reached me that you are
often to be seen drinking in bush hotels, which report
also says are often very, very low. You remember what
you asked me in Ferguson and Forester’s ten years ago.
It has often been borne in upon me that it is my mission
(same old item !) to save you. Now, if you give me
your word, which I know to be unbreakable, that you
will never again drink in a licensed house, I agree to
marry you. Can Ido more?—Yours prayerfully,
Helen m. Denman.
Chap, V. —The Answer.
Dear Nell,—Why you have taken ten years to fix up
your special mission is more than I can guess. I don’t
believe you love me as I loved you, as, in fact, I still love
you. Everyone will say one of two things—either that
you have thrown yourself at me, or that I am a fortune-
hunter. I know that you are now tremendously rich, and
I know that you will not draw back from your word. I
hope we shall be a happy couple. I vouch for one-half
and will do my best towards the other moiety. Telegraph
when you arrive in Melbourne. 1 shall meet you at
Blaokrock railway station, we shall drive to Curlyville
and there our old friend, Mr. Gilbert McMicking, wilj
weld us. I make the required vow from to-day.—Yours
as ever,
v J no. Wilson.
Chap. Vl.— The Travelling Dispensary.
John Wilson is awfully rich. He had made
money on his little station, and had bought up
one or two neighbours. Other land he had leased
(being a gentleman and not an M.L.C., he despised
dummyism), and seasons had been good. Then
came his wife’s great fortune, and with it—tem-
perance. _ Never again did Wilson breast a bar
or swig in a tap-room. People know him to
be a philanthropist. Where John goes there
also goes a huge, close waggon. It is fitted
inside with bottles and gallipots and lamps
and crucibles. The poor are always welcome to a
jiask from it. When visiting 1 tiny place where
hotel accommodation is necessary, that red van is
seen in the yard. The lame, the halt, and the
blind are invited to enter, and the barman is
always engaged as a dispenser. The upper ten,
also, impelled by curiosity or reason, go into the
front half and lounge and sit and smoke in it.
One of J. W.’s theories is the soothing effect of
music. The driver is a fiddler, and the company
sing : and Mr. Wilson actually pays the landlord
sixpence for every dose that the barman or bar-
maid dispenses. Mrs. Wilson has placed no veto
on a glass of wine at home, and in Melbourne or
bydney they put up in the best private furnished
house to be got, but John likes the bush, and goes
ü b u Ut doing good an . d his red shandrydan).
Hurry up, boys, Quicksilver and his missus arc
at the floyal. The peripatetic dispensary’s just
going into the yard. Fossick about for empty
bottles. This is a stroke of luck.”
SCOTTY THE WETNKLER.
Somehow the groveller seems to be always
devoid of grammar. In a shop window in Wel-
lington (M.L.), one of the tribe has on exhibition
a portrait of Victoria, and underneath it is an
explanation, which makes things more mysterious
than they were in their unexplained state :—-
Having had the pleasure of decorating, in my line of
business, Buckingham Palace for t his lady, our Queen for
five years, and having had the honour of siltin'* on’ her
Throne Chair, I feel bound in exhibiting the same"
Attached to this is another document of later
date, evidently intended to throw some li<dit
upon the explanation : —
It has been overheard, “We cannot see any throne
chair.” We can say that three-fourths of the people don’t
have a Show to see Inside the building, let alone to sit in
the Throne Chair, because there is our Queen’s soldiers
always On guard—the Life Guards, the Grecnadeers and
the Coldstreams ; only these noted regiments are allowed
None of the Volunteers.
After that, things are about as clear as a Mahatma
buried in a mud-hole.

At Brisbane, the other day, during the
hearing of the Q. Investment cases, Chief Justice
Lllley threatened to commit for contempt ”
witness Sir Thomas M'llwraith for refusing to
answer certain questions. The replies came.
THIS WILL SETTLE THE QUESTION.
“ I don’t believe there is a ivord of truth in it.”
That’s the way certain foolish people’talk when
they hear of anything unusual, or outside the
limits of their own observation or experience.
They are of the sort who laughed at Stephenson
when he introduced the railway, and at Morse
when he said that we could communicate by means
of electricity. Yet they don’t laugh at those
things now. They make use of them daily.
Some months ago the case of Mrs. Mary Cuddy
was first published. The great public accepted
the facts, as they had every reason to do. Others,
a very few (professional men perhaps) pretended
to doubt, and sent emissaries to inquire of the
lady herself whether her allegations had not been
added to, or altered, for the sake of popular effect.
Here_ is her answer ! She repeats what she at
first said, and puts a quietus on all who called
her words in question. It will be observed that
her statement is as plain and solemn as language
eau make it.
'[copy.]
I, Mary Cuddy, of 28, Catherine Street, Rich-
mond Road, Leeds, do solemnly and sincerely
declare as follows :—
Ever since I was a girl I have suffered from ill-
ness. I always had a pain both before and after
eating, and never seemed able to gain and keep
my strength, and felt that something was pulling
me down. I had a nastv queer feeling in my
stomach. _ Sometimes food seemed to ease it, and
at other times it made me feel worse, and often I
went without food, for I was afraid to eat. Com-
monly when food was placed before me I could not
touch it, and I often fainted at the very sight of it.
After a while I became so weak I could scarcely
stand or walk. I thought it was consumption
coming on by degrees and I took all sorts of
medicines to try and get relief, but it was of no use
and I got tired of taking physic, for I had lost all
faith in it. My business was so urgent that I was
compelled to be at work, otherwise I would have
laid in bed, so weak had I become. With the
weakness and loss of appetite there were other
feelings and signs that were bad and alarmed me
greatly. Among them were these A yellowish
colour of the skin and eyes, sometimes a cold,
clammy perspiration, pains and aches in the sides,
the chest and back, headache, a kind of wind or gas
coming up into my throat and mouth that was so
sour and sickening I could scarcely bear it. Once in
a while I would have a strange fluttering and pal-
pitation that made me think my heart must be
affected. My heart would thump so that I feared
it would jump out of its place, and I have had to
walk about the room for two or three hours at a
time, for I could not sit or lie. The pain was so
severe that I have asked my husband if he could
not hear my heart thumping as I walked about.
I always slept badly at night, and frequently had
horrible dreams, and was so melancholy and
depressed in spirits that I would sit down and
cry, for I got no pleasure as time dragged wearily
by. I had so little energy or strength that it was
all I could do to summon courage for the labour
upon which the family (at least in part) depended
for support. I am a dressmaker, and it will be
easilj 7 understood how hard my life was, for I
didn’t think it would last much longer. Not long
ago (May, 1887) I made up my mind to try a
medicine that is advertised and known all over
the country. I mean Mother Seigel’s Curative
Syrup. I had no faith in it at first, for how can
one believe in what one knows nothing about? I
bought and tried Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup
only because of its reputation. How could so
many people, I asked myself, praise a medicine so
much if it had no virtue? I can only say that I
found what they said to be true. After beginning
with the Syrup, relief soon followed. My food
digested better and gave me strength, and by
persevering with it all my pains disappeared. I
cotdd eat my food with a relish, and everything
agreed with me. Now and again when, through
confinement and hard work pi feel a touch of my
old gomplaint I take a dose or two of Seigel’s
Syrup and the trouble goes no further. Sinee the
publication of my testimonial many persons have
called at my house and asked me if all that is pub-
lished about my ease is true, and if the Proprietor's
of ScigeVs Syrup had made additions to my state-
ment.
lold them all that every word was true and
nothing had been added by the Proprietors of the
medicine, hut I could add a good deal more, for no
words can. describe what my sufferings were
during all those long years. I never expected
being well again in this world. Seigel’s Syrup
saved my life and I desire other sufferers to know
of what did so much for me. I will gladly answer
inquiries.
And I make this solemn declaration con-
scientiously believing the same to be true, by
virtue of the provisions of the Statutory Declara-
tion Act (Will. IV., c. C2.)
(Signed) Mary Cuddy.
Declared before mo at Leeds, in the
County of York, by the said Mary
Cuddy, on Monday, the 10th day of
August, 1891,
(Signed) Alf. Cooke,
Mayor o f Leeds. .
Not a syllable further is needed except to say
that her ailment. Indigestion and dyspepsia,
burdens and saddens the lives of many other
women (and men also), who will road with new
hope the outcome of Mrs. Cuddy's case, and place
a confidence which no cavilling can shake, in the
remedy which restored to her the health and
happiness that Providence designs for us all.
An elderly, prosperous - looking man
appeared, the other day, at Sydney Water
Court, to prosecute a young girl, residing
m a Sydney suburb, for having assaulted him.
He had been to the opera, he said, and was stand-
ing at the intersection of King and Elizabeth-
streets waiting for a homeward tram, when
defendant accosted him. Assuming that she
belonged to a certain class, he repulsed her,
saying he wanted nothing to do with her,
whereupon she struck him over the mouth
with her parasol, and then addressed her-
self to a constable. While the girl was speak-
ing to the officer the complainant gave her in
charge. Mr. Richards, for the defence, soon put
an entirely different complexion on the case.
His client, a handsome, lady - like person,
entered the box, and stated she had come
out of the Garrick Theatre with her younger
sister, and was waiting for a tram, when prose-
cutor asked her to go home with him. She
informed him that he had made a mistake, and
requested him not to molest her. He persisted in
his attentions, however, saying he knew her by
her voice, and offered her money. Exasperated
at the insult, she struck him across the face and
appealed to Constable Power, and while speaking
to,him the prosecutor gave her in charge. She
had never been in a court before in her life. The
officer named said he had been on that beat over
three years, and had never previously seen the
defendant. Mr. Richards said his client lived
with her mother, and offered to call evidence as
to her character, to show the improbability of her
having wantonly assaulted anyone in the street.
Mr. Delohery, S.M., said it was unnecessary to
call evidence. The case was a very bad one. The
charge would be dismissed, and the complainan
must pay a guinea costs. The plaintiff, who had
admitted to Mr. Richards that he was married,
immediately addressed the reporter nearest to
him, and asked that no mention might be made of
the case. The scribe referred him to the editor.
Strangely enough no report whatever appeared in
any one of the evening or morning papers. By
way of contrast may be mentioned a case which
came before the same court a few days afterwards,
in which a drunken, illiterate Swede, for assault
upon a married woman in George-street, was sent
to gaol for a month. The reports appear, in this
instance, with charming unanimity.
When O’Malley Clarke (now P.M. at
Sydney) received his first appointment in the
: N.S.W. public service as Clerk of Petty Sessions
at Moulamein—ever so many years ago-he
enquired where the place was, as he had never
heard of it before. At the Survey Office a
clerk pointed it out to him, very fine and largo,
on the map. “But how am I to get there?” lie
asked. “Ah, that we don’t know,” was the
reply. Nor was he more successful when he
enquired at the Colonial Secretary’s Office. He
then went to call upon Judge Therry, to whose
interest he owed the appointment-—in the first
place to thank him, and also to find out if pos-
sible how to reach the place of exile. “It's a
most exthraordinary thing,” said the Judge,
“but I saw the name a few minyutes ago in the
Herald.” The tipstaff was called and the paper
produced, and there, sure enough, it was—-“For
Moulmein direct, the well-known fast-sailing ship
, For freight, &c., apply to Gilchrist and
Alexander.” The worst of it was that the vessel
was to sail next day. “Niver moind,” said the
Judge, “ do ye just go down and give me compli-
ments to Misther Alexander and tell him ye’re a
frind of mine, an’ no doubt he’ll kape the ship for
a day.” O’Malley went to the office and the
thing was arranged, though the people at Gil-
christ’s were astonished at finding a passenger for
such a destination. The rest of the day was
spent in packing his traps (O’Malley hadn’t much
impedimenta in those days) and in wishing friends
good-bye. One of these said to him, “But how
are you going—will you ride or drive?” “Ride
or drive!” asked O’Malley with surprise—“I’m
going in one of Green’s ships.” “ It’s you that
are green, my boy,” said the friend ; “ why, how
on earth are you going by ship?” “Oh, that’s
all right,” insisted O’Malley, “and I sail in th e
morning.” His friend laughed and showed him
a map, and the future P.M. discovered that he
had booked a passage to Moulmein in India,
whereas he was bound to Moulamein on the
Edwards River. When he got there at last, lie
found that there was no courthouse, nor indeed
any sign of a township at all, except a bark
shanty and a blacksmith’s shop. He put up at
Eirebrace’s station, and used to go in every court
day and renew a notice which he fastened to a
dead stump—“Court adjourned until next Thurs-
day.”
The following interesting application wan
received recently by the manager of a Queensland
company. As a commentary on the auditing
business it is valuable : ”
I beg to make application to you for employment as
clerk in the service of your company. I have never had
much experience in clerical work any more than audit!no
accounts for divisional boards and secretaryships foil
various public institutions. 1
In other words he was quite good enough to audit
(for “various public institutions’’) but hadn’t
learned bookkeeping.
The Hawk wonders at Caringfcon, to all
appearances an intimate friend of Royalty
“posing so tremendously as the champion of the
great unwashed” . . , and making “an on-
slaught on Lord Knutsford which for bitterness
not to say vulgarity, has rarely been surpassed.”
Quite right, from the Hawk’s own Tory stand-
point. No man can serve two masters.
Dear Bulletin, —You remark that the
Irish, Welsh and Scotch comprised two-thirds of
the British army prior to the Crimean war ; if
you had said four-fifths you would perhaps have
been nearer the mark, for the Irish alone in the
“English” army in Spain numbered 25 per cent,
of the whole. There were always two courses
open to Paddy ; join the army or go to gaol; if he
didn’t like the gaol he could join the army, and if
he didn’t like the army he could go to gaol. Did
you ever notice, by the way, that it is always the
British army that gets well licked and the English
army that scores a glorious victory ?— Geokge
Bennett, Queensland.
